## 78

Dawn broke.

Rather than having slept in shifts, it would have been more accurate to say they had only lain down and closed their eyes. In such a state, not only were their minds utterly exhausted, but in the middle of the forest without bedding, they could hardly expect their physical fatigue to recover either.

(Maybe I should force them a little and hurry our return…)

Biting into the leftover meat they had grilled the night before for breakfast, the women set off with expressions that could not hide their fatigue.

Thanks to the representative woman, they somehow held together as a group, but if some fool started voicing complaints, I might have to step in.

However, the worry I had did not come to pass. On the contrary, perhaps due to the representative’s persuasion, they moved at a speed completely different from the day before.

The problem was that our route headed east, not south.

As I casually listened to their conversation, it seemed there was a supply depot for pioneer villages nearby, and they were heading there.

Not only did it store building materials, but it also held emergency supplies, so they were discussing securing provisions there before returning.

Originally, setting our course straight south would have led us right into the middle of the orc army besieging the city, so this route was more convenient. But more than that, it would allow them to take proper rest, which was significant.

If that was the reason, I accepted the course change—but as expected, the orcs had occupied the depot.

(I had wondered where they were getting tools for expanding their fortifications… It’s a cliché that enemies make good use of such things, but seeing it firsthand is a hassle.)

So I set down my luggage and promptly exterminated the orcs at the depot.

The women tried to stop me, but I ignored them.

The method was simple.

Charge straight in and kill everything in sight.

A total of eighty-eight orcs successfully transformed into mute lumps of meat.

The pigs that ran away were quickly hunted down and slaughtered as well.

Afterward, I simply gathered the corpses in one spot and left them there. Done.

The latter half of the work took more time, and I thought about asking for help, but given that the women were trained to handle guns, there was no way they could manage the weight of the orcs, so I gave up.

Obediently, I alone picked up the orcs and their remains and piled them in one place.

Then the representative woman approached me with a stern expression.

“What were you planning to do if they had called for reinforcements?”

Judging from how the others agreed with her, it seemed they had no grasp of my abilities.

I exaggerated a weary sigh to make it obvious I was annoyed, then went to retrieve my luggage.

When I returned, the representative woman was briskly giving orders and working hard to secure supplies.

Since I had already cleaned my dirty hands and feet, I took out paper and pen from my backpack and began writing a note.

“Don’t assume my detection range is on a human level. There were no signs of orcs nearby. If there had been any chance of escape, I wouldn’t have initiated the attack. Furthermore, given the orcs’ sense of smell, it was only a matter of time before they detected you. There was no choice but to eliminate them.”

Even after showing them this, they still did not seem convinced. As expected, in a monster’s form, neither trust nor understanding was easy to come by.

It was a natural reaction, but given how often I had been dealing with elves lately, I couldn’t help but feel a little disappointed by the contrast.

Perhaps soldiers were simply more rigid in their thinking compared to mercenaries or hunters.

Until the necessary supplies were gathered, I kept watch outside the rows of warehouses in the depot.

Nothing happened in particular, and no orcs appeared, so the work ended smoothly, and we departed.

I had some slight concerns about continuing without rest, but it seemed their priority was to get out of orc territory as quickly as possible.

With no choice, I followed them, checked the route, and moved ahead to lead.

Now that we were traveling across open plains, our speed should have been faster, but perhaps due to being overly cautious with no cover, they sometimes stopped in their tracks.

Repeating that kind of march naturally threw off our schedule.

Again today, we camped in an awkward halfway spot.

At this rate, I began to worry that we might run out of water first.

(So these are new recruits, huh… Seems quite different from how things were in the Empire.)

Perhaps their training period had been greatly reduced?

If that was the case, then the Furon Council’s front lines were in quite a precarious state.

Part of me thought it might be better to leave them and rush there instead.

(No, that won’t do. If I abandon them here and they somehow make it back on their own, my position will be in jeopardy.)

While thinking, “I’ve picked up quite a troublesome burden,” I also considered the reward for successfully protecting these women who were hardly different from civilians.

The previous contract was only with the women I had rescued, not with the Furon military.

There would be nothing wrong with demanding some sort of reward.

But even with that in mind, if I didn’t take some measures, we might face an ending like “the town you returned to had already fallen.”

Thus, the next day—

“From today, you will match your pace to ours. If you cannot, we will leave you behind. Based on your actions so far, we have determined that you are not critical to our objective.”

The representative’s face changed color when she read the note.

“What… do you mean?”

“From your actions, it is extremely doubtful you have any influence with your superiors. Therefore, due to your lowered importance, we decided to reduce unnecessary effort.”

I showed her a memo I had prepared in advance for the answer I expected.

“Wait, I—”

“The decision will not change. Either follow us and gain safety, or return on your own.”

I showed her another memo as she was lost in thought.

I had anticipated several scenarios last night and prepared multiple replies, so the conversation progressed smoothly.

“Think of it this way. Right now, you have me as combat strength here. Would rushing to bring me back be a plus—or a minus?”

In the end, she chose to follow me.

When she announced this decision, agitation spread among the others, but the representative somehow managed to suppress it.

Their marching speed visibly increased, but I still had them push their limits regarding stamina.

At this pace, we might make contact with the enemy as early as today.

(On the battlefield, saying ‘I’ve reached my limit’ means death. So if you are soldiers, walk as if your lives depended on it.)

As I kept walking, thinking that, just as expected, around noon I spotted a thin plume of rising smoke.

“No way, is that the city of Brof!?”

Reading the note I showed her, the representative woman involuntarily raised her voice.

They were still too far away to see it themselves, but the chances were high that a battle was underway right now.

It was only one plume of smoke, but who knew what it would look like up close?

I thought to suggest we hurry, but before that, the representative ordered everyone to drop their baggage and start running.

“Depending on the situation, I will engage and draw the enemy’s attention. You all should circle around and return.”

When I showed the note I had written, the representative nodded, and upon reading it, the women were suddenly filled with motivation, a complete change from before.

We dropped our backpacks and sprinted across the plain. As the number of rising smoke plumes increased one by one—finally, a part of the orc army came into view.

At the same time, I spotted the siege engines the orcs were using, and once their numbers became clear to the naked eye, the sheer quantity made me want to look away.

(There’s definitely over five thousand. It’s fortunate they’re aiming for a single-point breakthrough without encircling, but for those under attack, that’s no consolation.)

For now, I conveyed that I would charge into the left flank and wreak havoc as planned, and increased my speed.

If they could circle around and reach a gate on the opposite side of where the orcs were, the mission would be complete.

Following them to the very end would be safest, but the situation wasn’t looking very good.

I could occasionally see orcs who had climbed the city walls being struck down and falling, which meant time was of the essence.

The representative seemed to understand that; she nodded after reading my memo and changed course to circle around the battlefield.

My detection ability wasn’t picking up any orcs, so it should be fine.

The rest depended on their luck.

As I ran straight toward the orc encampment, I got close enough to visually make out the leading—or in this case, rearmost—ranks, and that’s when I was spotted.

With a “Buo-oo” horn sound, about five heavily armed orcs moved forward to block my path.

Up on the city wall, one of the soldiers who had been pushing back the orcs spotted me and pointed.

It seemed there was still some leeway—good. In that case, I would rampage to my heart’s content.

Starting with these heavily armed orcs.

Facing the pigs wielding heavy weapons like battle axes and mauls, I kept running without slowing down at all.

(Now, let me show you the violence of mass!)

Ignoring the perfectly timed downward swing of the battle axe, I accelerated even more and, at the moment of impact, snapped my neck upward.

Before it could swing its weapon, the heavily armored orc was sent flying, and without even sparing it a glance, I charged straight into the herd of pigs.

Before the sudden monster that appeared behind them, the orcs were trampled with no chance to resist—and whatever they felt watching that, I didn’t care. Seeing the opportunity, the humans on the city walls launched their counterattack.

At that point, there was no way the orcs would be climbing the walls anymore.

“GAAAAAAHHHH!”

I roared.

To flaunt my presence, to carve fear into the pigs, I roared loudly.

(O my beloved descendants of the Imperial citizens, don’t show me such pathetic sights!)

My tension reached its peak.

While being careful not to overdo it, I decided to rampage to my heart’s content.

+++

## 79

To put it bluntly, it was a massacre—or just pest control.

No matter how many armed pigs you gathered, a pig was still a pig.

Even if they braced their shields, I neither stopped nor could be stopped.

Right now, an orc that had planted its heavy shield firmly before my swinging arm was sent flying through the air.

I could have batted it sideways, but that would only make it crash into the other pigs and stop right away.

By giving it some airtime like this, I could force the orcs at the predicted crash site to move.

That disrupted their formation and caused confusion.

With such a densely packed mass, confused troops would simply crush each other to death on their own.

It was a useful tactic to save effort, though I felt a twinge of disappointment that punching them to death was surprisingly inefficient.

Men sometimes communicated with their fists.

By the way, I avoided kicking them much, since they tended to burst when I did.

While hurling orcs, I glanced at the city walls; it looked like they had firmly regained the advantage, with far fewer pigs clinging to them.

The small fires caused by flaming arrows were also extinguished, so it was safe to say the defense had succeeded.

The only question was when these pigs would retreat—but since that was a hassle, I decided to pay a visit to where their commander was likely to be.

The women I rescued should already have moved to a safe zone; this seemed like a good point to bring down the curtain.

Swaggering through the orc horde as if walking across an empty field, I mowed down any pig that came at me and pushed straight toward the center.

Spears snapped, axes shattered, shields and armor became meaningless.

Before me—like a nightmare—appeared one fatso.

Not a big orc—an actual fat one.

It was taller than the others, but its girth was in a different league: an undeniable, massive blob.

Drooling, clutching an oversized meat cleaver, and with unfocused eyes, it gurgled “gup-gup.”

My first thought was, “Is it high on drugs?” and it probably was.

Large-scale orc groups started using medicinal herbs.

I recalled many cases where they were seen using something akin to narcotics, either to overcome fear or temporarily boost themselves.

(Judging by its size, maybe this one has the most raw power?)

Thinking I’d test its strength, I ignored the cleaver swinging down and grabbed its hand—only to crush it to pulp by accident from sheer force.

It seemed it wasn’t even worthy of a contest.

With no choice, I wrenched away the huge meat cleaver and lopped off the fatso’s head.

Blood gushed as it fell backward, and I could clearly see fear spreading among the surrounding orcs.

I scoffed, “Finally starting to understand the difference in strength?” but it was still too early for them to run.

Unless I properly demonstrated my strength a bit more, it could interfere with my future activities.

I needed to firmly imprint that they were the hunted. Otherwise, thanks to idiots who wouldn’t flee and instead charged in, I might end up killing too many by mistake.

If they didn’t maintain their numbers appropriately and remain enough of a threat to deter humans from venturing into the forest, my operational territory would only keep shrinking.

I wanted them to remain tools to be used until the very end.

That wish was embedded in this slaughter.

(But they’re holding out longer than I thought. A normal orc force would’ve collapsed long ago…)

For their morale to hold after so much killing, either they’d been enjoying great spoils, or their commander—or leader—was terrifying.

I concluded they wouldn’t retreat unless I struck the head, and continued pushing toward the center, searching for a strong-looking orc.

The meat cleaver in my hand couldn’t withstand my power and broke after three swings, so I repurposed it as a throwing weapon.

I cleared out all the pigs blocking my path and reached the central area, but still hadn’t found the presumed commander.

With nothing better to do, I killed time grabbing random pigs and tossing them around until the orc ranks split apart.

Through the gap slowly strode a fully armored orc—its aura clearly different even from a distance. It was the size of an ogre, no doubt about it.

A glance at the city walls showed no more orcs trying to climb; they’d withdrawn out of bow range, gathering around as if to watch this battle.

It had turned into a monster-versus-monster showdown, ignoring the humans entirely, which could make negotiations later a bit troublesome.

Being too strong had its downsides.

Standing upright on two legs, the ogre-class orc was taller than me, who walked on all fours.

I had to look up slightly, but so what?

I could understand the romance of dual-wielding greatswords, but that plate armor was unacceptable.

If you were going to face me, you should take that armor off and fight.

I exhaled in disappointment, as if to say, “This won’t be a worthy opponent.”

Perhaps disliking my attitude, it pointed one of its greatswords at me and grunted something incomprehensible.

(I don’t get you, baldy. In the end, you’re still just an orc… I’d hoped for an opponent with some bite.)

I stepped forward, casually entering baldy’s range.

It swung at the same moment, and I was surprised by the speed.

It was much faster than I had expected, enough that I reflexively blocked it with my arm, but the impact wasn’t bad at all.

If its weapon had been a magic sword capable of cutting me, it might have had a chance, but unfortunately, with a heavy weapon that could only chop, the best it could do was leave a scratch.

Still, that one strike was praiseworthy.

So I stepped in again and, as a little payback, threw a quick, speed-focused jab at baldy’s face.

Unable to dodge, it took the blow squarely on its snout and staggered back a step, but quickly rekindled its fighting spirit and launched a flurry of blows with both greatswords.

I knocked each wild swing aside with my fists, caring nothing for its sword forms.

The sound of metal clanging against rock-like fists echoed for a while, until I saw panic appear on baldy’s face.

Its breathing grew ragged, and the speed of its swings gradually slowed.

Its limit came far too soon; I sighed in disappointment and decided to end this farce that hadn’t even qualified as a fight.

I batted aside the greatswords alternately with wide swings, leaving its torso wide open, then spun my body.

Hooked its legs with my tail, yanked them out from under it, and as its massive body floated slightly in the air, I unleashed an almost full-power roundhouse kick into its belly.

What kind of sound did that make?

At the very least, what the surrounding orcs heard was an explosion.

The kick smashed through the armor, destroyed the ogre-like body, and sent pieces flying.

The meat chunks and armor fragments landed on another orc, crushing it, but that was trivial by now.

The orc army broke at that very moment.

After a brief silence to comprehend—or rather, accept—the reality, they began fleeing all at once.

But there would be no pursuit.

Because I had no interest, and because my presence alone prevented the humans from chasing.

For now, the smell and stares here were getting uncomfortable, so I lumbered off to retrieve my luggage.

The Furon side would likely need some time as well, so I decided I’d get myself cleaned up before paying them a visit.

If time allowed, the women I rescued should be able to explain things.

For the sake of smooth progress—for the best outcome—I would endure for now.

A glance up at the walls showed some soldiers looking frustrated at the fleeing orcs, while others simply rejoiced at having survived.

The majority—no, almost all of them—would only think of the earlier clash as a “fight between monsters.”

(For now, just be happy you ‘luckily survived.’)

I slowly walked with Brof at my back.

But then an uneasy thought crossed my mind.

(…There’s no way the fleeing orcs will steal my luggage, right?)

As I walked, lost in thought, before I knew it, I had started running.

Someone on the wall shouted when they saw that.

It was understandable—they’d be startled if the object of their vigilance suddenly bolted.

I offered a silent apology and ran toward where I’d left my luggage.

Please don’t let it have been stolen.

+++

## 80

Relieved that my belongings were intact, I let out a sigh of relief and used some water I had taken out to wash off the splattered blood.

After checking the contents and confirming nothing had been stolen, I considered preparing some responses in advance in my notebook and picked up a pen.

However, there weren’t many questions I could predict.

I already had notes prepared for the usual questions, fully intending to reuse them, so there wasn’t much need.

I jotted down a few that might work for different scenarios, but I couldn’t shake the feeling that it would end up being pointless, and my motivation flagged.

With little else to do, I needed a different way to kill time.

With no choice, I went hunting, but unsurprisingly, after that many orcs had moved through, I couldn’t find any big game at all.

After nearly two hours, the only catch was a single rabbit—by far a new personal worst record.

Well, at least it killed some time, so I headed toward the city.

As soon as the city walls came into view, there was visible commotion on the other side.

As I lumbered closer, I spotted soldiers on the walls pointing at me here and there.

The number of people gathering on top of the walls kept increasing; it was clear they were on high alert.

(Hmm… does this mean the word hasn’t reached them down there?)

As I got closer, I could make out their movements even without using any enhanced sight.

I spotted them readying crossbows and muskets to greet me.

Just what had the rescued women been doing?

Assuming something happened, I considered the possible scenarios.

They dismissed any negotiations with a monster; thus, nothing was said.

The information was suppressed before reaching command.

They couldn’t make it back and became orc food again…

Those were the three that came to mind immediately.

(Ah, there’s also the possibility that “they reported it but weren’t taken seriously.”)

That one was starting to feel the most likely.

No matter how much communication was possible, I was still a monster.

And right now, the Furon Council was fighting monsters called orcs.

As I gazed distantly and thought, “Maybe it was too much to hope for,” a gunshot rang out—the bullet struck the ground right in front of me.

For the moment, I reached out to see what kind of ammunition they were using, dug the bullet out of the ground, and sighed when I saw the deformed round lead ball.

I had expected it, but the technological decline still hit hard.

To think that the Empire, once so glorious it seemed at its peak, had descendants using weapons like these—it made me want to weep.

I exhaled deeply and stood still.

It wasn’t disappointment per se, but seeing how they responded to me raised the uneasy thought that perhaps no remnants of the Empire remained.

If it had been the Empire I remembered, the citizens would have gone into high gear shouting things like, “Whoa, what’s with that monster!? It can talk!? What the heck!?”

They would have stormed the government halls for the fun of it, demanding, “This looks interesting, give us more updates!” simply out of boredom.

Then the mass media would have filmed them, the news would have gone viral, and before I knew it, I’d have starred on TV.

Then maybe I’d have ended up on a slightly risqué show, there’d have been a wardrobe malfunction, and—

(Aaaaaaaah! Why? Why has everything declined this much!?)

The frustration nearly made me want to chew a handkerchief, but thankfully, my emotion-suppression function kicked in.

My emotions had gotten far too messy, so I was grateful for it.

Given how far my thoughts had gone off track, it seemed the shock before calming down had been quite severe.

At any rate, since they were firing, I couldn’t just not respond.

As a monster, there were parts of my pride I couldn’t back down from.

If I let them underestimate me, some fool was sure to do something stupid.

To prevent that, I’d have to make them accept some losses.

Besides, since they’d shot, they should have the resolve to deal with the consequences.

“GAAAAAAAARRR!”

I lowered my backpack and let out a roar before sprinting toward the walls.

A volley of gunfire erupted, but since I had increased my speed right before, none of it hit.

A few grazed me, but my body wasn’t weak enough for that to matter; even if they had hit directly, it wouldn’t have caused significant damage.

I leaped at the ten-meter-high walls, kicked off them to climb higher, and reached out with my arms.

Before they could even react, I had climbed atop the walls. Muskets spat fire at me, but my tough hide blocked them, leaving only an itchy warmth.

I even found myself scratching my belly where a bullet hit—it was that harmless. At least the power was definitely above a crossbow’s.

For the hardest parts of my body—shoulders and back—it felt no more than “Oh, something bounced off.”

For now, I smacked the nearest soldier behind me with my tail and grabbed the one in front of me.

Being shot from behind wasn’t painful or itchy, so I ignored it.

But being shot in the front—even if the belly was fine, I wanted to avoid the face.

So I used the captured soldier as a shield while steadily walking down the narrow wall.

The soldiers packed tightly together hurriedly fell back, but the path jammed and caused a traffic block.

I didn’t want anyone dying from a ten-meter fall, so I reached out, grabbed soldiers, and tossed them back to clear the jam.

The ones I threw screamed pitifully, but even an adult would lose control of their legs being tossed around at this height.

And so, clearing obstacles, I arrived at the top of the city gate.

Unfortunately, my massive body meant I couldn’t go inside, but from outside I grasped the basic structure.

(Hmm… maybe their technology is slightly better than Canaan’s?)

I hadn’t seen Canaan’s tech directly, so I couldn’t be sure, but I had a rough estimate based on intuition.

While casually swatting aside obstructing soldiers with my tail and flicking gun-wielders away with a finger snap, I observed Furon’s reaction. The gunfire ceased, likely because they realized their attacks weren’t working.

I thought, “Great, now I won’t have to scratch,” but then—

“Aim! Volley fire!”

At the officer’s order, over a dozen muzzles flashed.

Reacting to the voice, I covered my face with both arms, but the bullets concentrated on my chest and belly, making me so itchy that I dropped my guard to scratch all over.

In front of the dumbstruck soldiers realizing it wasn’t working at all, I made a show of letting out a huge yawn.

“Don’t mock us, you monster!”

An older man, apparently the commander, drew his saber and charged at me alone.

He ordered his troops to retreat, so it was likely a decoy, a suicidal distraction.

Like he was trying to throw away the resolve of this dashing bearded middle-aged man, I caught his full-force strike with two fingers, removed his hat with my free hand, and found his scalp bald.

Feeling sorry, I quietly put his hat back and gently patted the shoulder of the man futilely trying to wrestle his saber free from me.

For some reason, he took offense, let go of the saber, and swung a fist at me.

Naturally, punching me only hurt his hand, so I ignored it. Behind him, soldiers who couldn’t fire with him in the way stared on with troubled expressions.

I had intended to mess with them a bit more and make them hurt, but I decided to forgive them here out of respect for this man’s bravery.

However, the man himself got too heated, and I looked up at the sky, wondering what to do.

Given the time that had passed, some people began realizing “Something’s not right.”

More and more soldiers stopped attacking and just watched; only the older man’s shouting echoed.

At first he was yelling things like, “A monster like you!” but maybe his fists had reached their limit—he started kicking my shins while shouting, “What do you know about my problems!?” venting about his bald spot.

Once again, I patted his shoulder gently as if to comfort him.

Then he picked up his dropped saber and slashed, but it snapped cleanly, so I let it slide.

For now, I decided to use this older man as my contact window and abducted him.

Holding him, I jumped off the walls and ran to where I had left my belongings.

The poor man’s hat flew off somewhere as he made pitiful noises. I set him down and pulled out my prepared notebook from my backpack, thrusting it before his eyes.

“I freed thirty-eight women captured by the orcs and escorted them back here. Do you know about this?”

The older man’s eyes widened as he read the note.

“No way… you understand our language?”

I nodded at his mutter and showed the next note.

“I want to talk with your commanding officer. I secured a promise to arrange such a meeting in exchange for escorting and returning those women.”

“Wait, what’s going on!?”

The older man shouted after reading the note, but I ignored him and wrote a new one.

“It’s exactly as it says. I raided the orc fort in the rear, freed thirty-eight captive women, and negotiated with them. The result was a promise to arrange a meeting with your commanding officer—or someone equivalent—on the condition that I escorted them safely back to this city.”

Reading it, the older man muttered, “I can’t believe it…” but I wasn’t sure which part he meant.

(Maybe all of it, but this is probably a normal reaction when dealing with a monster.)

Though he looked at me suspiciously, since I’d spared his life, he likely believed at least “there’s some truth mixed in.”

“For now, I’d like you to go back. I’ll wait here until sunset.”

I handed over the note and let the older man return.

He looked back at me several times on his way, but I ignored him and sat down on the spot.

I sighed, hoping this would lead to some progress.

At the same time, I realized there was still quite a bit of time until sunset and regretted, thinking, “Shoot, maybe I should have set a tighter time limit.”

+++

## 81

Not knowing how long it would take, I first butchered the rabbit I had caught.

I dug a hole to bury the discarded parts, built a hearth, washed my hands, and prepared the iron plate.

Lighting the fuel I had brought, I quickly smeared fat on the plate and placed the cut meat on it.

I carefully sprinkled salt and pepper, listening closely so as not to miss the timing to flip the meat.

The soldiers on the castle wall were bewildered by the monster suddenly starting a barbecue, but I was too busy grilling to care.

These days it was becoming something of an art form, so even a slight mistake was unacceptable.

And so, I was focusing when I heard the sound of the castle gate opening.

Reflexively looking over, I saw about five people walking toward me.

One was the old man from earlier, and the rest were two men and two women.

Since they were all armed, I couldn’t tell who the representative was.

Using an iron skewer, I deftly flipped the meat and lightly sprinkled salt and pepper on the cooked side.

(Now that I think about it, there’s also the option of squeezing citrus on it. I’ll have to get some next time, wherever I can.)

My attention drifted a little toward the meat, but I still kept them in sight, so there was no problem.

One of them pointed at me grilling and laughed, apparently realizing what I was doing.

She looked like someone I could talk to, I thought as I prepared some dishes.

The other three looked at me with disgust, while the remaining woman’s expressionless face made her stand out and easy to remember.

As they got closer, even while watching the meat I could make out their features.

The men were easy to read: one a burly, obvious soldier, the other a bearded, pot-bellied bigwig—let’s skip describing the old man.

The women were the expressionless slender one and the laughing voluptuous one.

My guess was they were Number Two and Number Four, or maybe Number Five.

Given the military uniforms, I couldn’t be certain—that was a shortcoming in my training.

“Hey, I hear you want to talk with us,”

The glamorous blonde beauty in uniform spoke, standing before me as I grilled.

Pants weren’t bad, but if she’d been in a miniskirt it would’ve been even better.

I looked up thinking that—and my thoughts froze.

I simply reached out my hand without saying anything.

Two of them immediately raised their guns in response, but she held them back with one hand and, standing tall, looked straight at me without even glancing at my extended arm.

I took off her cap; her semi-long blonde hair swayed slightly in the wind.

(…Ha, this is beyond uncanny.)

Spitting image—it wouldn’t be an exaggeration to call her the living double of my sister.

If her hair were a little longer, I might have hugged her on the spot.

Overcome by nostalgia, emotions almost welled up, but suddenly it was as if a switch flipped, and I returned to calm.

I put her cap back and flipped the meat.

Taking out my notepad, I wrote:

“Felt like I’d seen you somewhere before. My apologies.”

“Oh my, monsters these days hit on people, huh?”

At her words after reading the note, the three behind her waved their hands in denial, like saying, “No, that’s not it.”

Taking it as an invitation to banter, I played dumb in a monster-like way: “What does ‘hit on’ mean?”

“It means trying to pick up the opposite sex. So, what did you want to talk about?”

Her tone became slightly more casual, but with her voice overlapping with my sister’s, the resemblance only grew stronger.

Before searching for the relevant page in my notebook, I cut a piece of meat and held it out to her.

She popped it into her mouth without hesitation, complaining, “A bit tough,” as she swallowed it down.

“I want information gathered by humans about the orcs.”

“No. I don’t know what your goal is.”

She refused the moment she saw the note.

(An immediate answer, huh? Between her unhesitating bite of meat and this boldness, she’s definitely from my bloodline.)

Now that I thought about it, Mother was like this too, and my sister resembled her a lot.

My younger sister had been a bit shy, but when she steeled herself, she reminded me of Mother as well.

Cutting off a bite for myself, I opened the page I’d prepared and showed it to her.

“Right now, the forest orcs are increasing too much. I want as accurate a count and their locations as possible.”

“That’s why I said no. Tell me your purpose. Your purpose.”

As I struggled with the tiny notebook in my hand, she suddenly asked, “Ah, can you slice that part thinly?”—of all things, a request for more meat.

I paused, filleted a piece of meat into two, and seared the red interior on the iron plate.

“They’ve increased far too much. I judged some culling is necessary. For that, we request the information humans have.”

“‘Request’? …Do you even know what that word means?”

She made no attempt to hide her displeasure upon reading the note, but this was among the scenarios I had anticipated.

I hoped she’d forgive the slight semantic overreach, as I checked the doneness of the meat and wrote a new line in my notepad.

“I see, you understand words. You just can’t speak, right?”

At her question, I let out a goofy “Guaaah,” and she started laughing.

“So, you want to cull the orcs because there are too many. That’s why you want their numbers and locations, right?”

I nodded to her words and skewered a piece of grilled meat to offer her.

She brushed aside her blonde hair with one hand, bit the meat off the skewer, and chewed.

Even as a beauty, her slightly wild way of eating suited her.

(Sis was like this too—anything she did looked picturesque. With her great figure, it was chaos in the streets with guys flocking around.)

I recalled once being punched due to a misunderstanding, and as an apology she’d treated me to expensive meat.

“Hmm… but that raises one question. Why are you culling the overgrown orcs? And since you want numbers and positions, you intend to leave some alive, right?”

“Extermination isn’t the goal. I want to reduce their numbers and maintain the forest’s balance.”

After reading my prepared note, her gaze turned sharp.

“Would you say the same if humans started developing the forest?”

Showing her the page that said “Depends on the situation and place,” she seemed to ponder for a moment.

Crossing her arms pressed her ample chest together, but because she looked too much like my sister, I couldn’t see her that way.

“Then… how much would you allow?”

Oh, I hadn’t expected that question.

Or rather, I didn’t think she’d ask so specifically.

Put bluntly, asking that could be seen as drawing a “line in the sand.”

I didn’t know how much territorial ambition the current Furon Council had, but I couldn’t imagine they weren’t thinking about regaining the old lands.

This fell under “politically awkward questions.”

The old man’s openly troubled expression showed he understood that much.

I exhaled deeply and scrawled on the memo paper.

“Beyond the fortress I destroyed, there’s farmland. For now, be satisfied with that area.”

She looked at the note and raised her cheek in a grin.

Seeing her face reminded me of my sister, and I could see what she was thinking.

“I see. I suspected there was a reason the orcs could maintain their increased numbers, but I didn’t have confirmation.”

To my additional note, she smiled and said, “Correct.”

In other words, she was also saying:

“We hardly have any information on the orcs.” —This was one of the worst-case scenarios I had anticipated.

Her next line was easy to guess.

“There’s one thing I want to ask… Since you understand language and appear before us like this—can I assume negotiation is possible?”

Getting too close was no good.

My combat power was something a nation would desperately want.

I couldn’t sell myself cheaply.

Even if the Furon Council carried Imperial blood, I couldn’t allow myself to be conveniently used.

I “belonged to the Fullretos Empire,” not the Council-State.

That was the boundary I had drawn for myself.

I couldn’t overturn it.

Thus, I wouldn’t engage in unbalanced deals.

“Forget it. If you intend to rely on me, I demand an equivalent price. And you can’t prepare compensation worthy of my work. Therefore, no negotiation.”

If they had information, I’d take it and act on my own.

Even without it, I’d act on my own.

If they had information and wanted me involved, a joint front wasn’t impossible.

But if they had no information and just wanted to rely on me—that was no different from hiring a mercenary.

My combat strength exceeded moving an entire army.

Then the reward had to be immense, if not on the scale of a national budget.

It wasn’t the same as mercenaries asking for help.

Since it was the military asking, I had to respond accordingly.

Perhaps understanding what I meant, she sighed and raised her hands in a gesture of surrender.

With those resentful eyes, she asked, “Are you really a monster?” but seemed to have given up on using me.

(So gallant… truly feels like my sister’s descendant.)

She shone a little too brightly.

I wanted her to be alive too.

But that was that; this was this.

If there was no information to gain, there was no point in staying here.

I quickly finished the grilled meat, put out the fire, and started cleaning up.

“…There’s only one piece of information I can provide.”

I didn’t stop cleaning but silently listened.

Either way, if I was going to reduce orcs, it was likely a plea to hunt some pigs somewhere.

“The orcs have been increasing for quite a while… but they only started intruding into human territory a few years ago. From that time, a certain individual has been sighted repeatedly.”

That wasn’t what I’d expected—it sounded pretty important.

“We call it the ‘Emperor.’ Not a King, but a higher-ranking individual. Based on sightings—it’s probably a monster on par with or even above you.”

+++

## 82 - A Former Shrine Maiden’s Perspective (1)

Inside a building in a certain village, a lone woman spoke before the gathered crowd.

There was no way to know whether this choice would turn out to be the right one.

At the very least, she was certain he had intelligence and was an existence with whom communication was possible.

Whether her father and mother had simply been caught up in a madman’s schemes, or whether it had been the sorrowful result of believing in a faint hope—this was surely necessary to decide that.

That was why she said, she was moving now.

Rusheru Forshna continued:

“If you wish to remain afraid of contamination that may or may not even exist, then stay. However, I highly value those who are willing to sacrifice themselves for the future. We do not know what kind of monsters we will encounter if we proceed deep into the forest. We might not be able to return to this place. At worst, none of us may come back. Even so, I believe we must go for the sake of the future. If you are one who says ‘I will go,’ then state your name.”

At her words, members of the clan stood up one by one.

When the number reached ten, Rusheru stopped another who was about to stand with a gesture of her hand, ending it there.

These were those confident in their own strength—the brave warriors of Forshna, counted among the Three Great Clans.

There was surely no room for fear among them.

“Well then, I entrust the guidance to you.”

Turning toward me, Rusheru gave an “order” with a smile of “I’m counting on you.”

With dead eyes, I—Ashiru, the former shrine maiden who had just been accepted into the Forshna clan—muttered a small “Yes.”

The group advanced into the dark forest.

At the lead was Princess Rusheru, giving an impossible order to a person like me with no stamina or magic to proceed through the forest. The group walked in the direction specified with strict caution.

Around the point where we crossed the river, one of the warriors on watch discovered a game trail.

It was concluded that its owner was without a doubt Argos, and since it roughly matched the direction of the base I remembered, we followed this trail.

“Most likely, this was made when Argos went to the river. Ashiru, according to your testimony, he catches fish and eats them, right?”

I nodded at the warrior’s words as he returned from scouting the surroundings.

“From the information, he’s basically omnivorous. His sense of taste is said to be close to the human species, and he prefers to use fire to cook his food… quite the bizarre monster.”

The older warrior who constantly kept a watchful eye by Rusheru’s side muttered this, and several others nodded in agreement.

I also agreed with that opinion.

I wanted to pay that lumpy bastard back at least once for tricking and mocking me, but sadly, in my current state, that was an impossible wish.

(What’s with that whole “No mistreatment” thing! Or all the “You should at least be able to do that” and “Why can’t you even do something like this?” talk! Yes, fine, I’m an indoor type! But assuming that means I can handle housework is not okay! People have strengths and weaknesses!)

Gritting my teeth to move my weary legs forward, I cursed in my heart.

Ever since being taken under Forshna clan’s protection, it had been nothing but days of relentless training.

There was none of the ideal daily life I had imagined; with the fatigue accumulating by the day, I had tried to run away several times.

However, that princess said this to poor pitiful me:

“Oh my, are you going outside? Zesato’s people are waiting with baited breath, you know?”

Run, and you die.

Immediately understanding that, I trudged back to the prepared room.

And then more training—thanks to the princess’s gracious words, At the very least, learn to do all household chores, I began days of being drilled by the maids serving Forshna. Now I was being bullied under the guise of “training” all day long.

“Big breasts but useless, huh…”

“Is this like false advertising?”

“How did you even survive to that age?”

On and on, I was insulted until my heart nearly broke.

So I made a direct appeal to Lady Rusheru.

(The princess is obsessed with that monster. Then, as the elf who got closer to him than anyone, I can be useful in that area!)

And this was the result.

This cold treatment, as if saying ‘We can see right through your thoughts.’

I’d suspected as much, but could it be that the princess was only sheltering me for the sake of her dealings with Argos?

The fact that no further news about Zesato ever reached my ears, among other things, made me feel like things weren’t going the way I had hoped.

The same with this “contamination” business.

They spoke about things our bottom-tier clan had never been told as if it were only natural.

Decisions were being made without my knowledge; frankly, I could feel the blatant disregard for me dripping through.

Where had my worth gone?

When we arrived at his dwelling on the second day and everyone was left speechless in surprise, I basked in superiority, only to be urged, “Hurry up and guide us.”

“Your work starts here. Really, do you know how much we were delayed because of you? Work hard enough to make up for it.”

All the men of this clan were cold to me.

Apparently, they were all interested in the princess and didn’t spare me a glance.

As for the women, I could hear their bitter mutters of “useless fatty meat.”

If I dared say something like “The voice of jealousy is music to my ears,” hellish training awaited me.

Just you wait, I told myself, revising my future plans.

“Oh, there’s an underground.”

“It’s starting to look like ruins.”

“There aren’t any traps, right?”

The men in the lead examined the door as they spoke their thoughts, listening to my explanations.

When I explained the place where we had meals, the two women began inspecting the dishes and cooking utensils with great interest.

“As expected, the items around here are from the old Empire.”

“It doesn’t seem like something from around here… did he bring them from somewhere else?”

They whispered in hushed tones, apparently confirming something, but was there really anything worth seeing here?

Sitting on a collapsed wall, I stretched my tired legs to rest, when I was called over.

“Yes, yes, what is it now?”

Swaying unsteadily, I went over to the men examining around the door and was handed an old sheet of paper.

“You can read the Furon Council-State script, right? Read this for us.”

Apparently translation was needed.

Surely now my evaluation would become more accurate—or so I thought, but there were a lot of words I didn’t understand, maybe technical terms, and I couldn’t grasp the content well.

“Uh, this is from Imperial times, so there are a lot of words not used in the modern day and the meaning is…”

In the end, even when asking others who knew the Furon language, we got the same answer, but we did manage to grasp the general idea.

It seemed to be records of exchanges of information called classified data, and what they contained.

It was decided to take them back as valuable materials, but the princess strictly ordered that we bring back as little as possible.

The reason was that taking too much might inspire suspicion, but would he even care about such things?

Now that I thought about it, I seemed to recall there being some materials like that, better kept secret.

While I pondered this and that, we ended up going underground, and I got sandwiched between the two women and fell.

I let out a pathetic cry, but let me note that my landing was perfect.

Well, as expected, Argos wasn’t here.

One examined the bedding, the others examined nearby rooms.

As for me, I had nothing left to say, so I staggered around looking at the area.

But I didn’t go deeper inside.

At that time, I found something I didn’t remember.

A board was hung on the wall with papers pinned to it.

Did something like this exist? I thought idly as I approached and read what was written.

“Coming back? Are you bored?”

This was probably a message for me.

Turning over the paper, there was writing on the back too.

“Flipping it over? Are you stupid?”

Lowering my gaze a little, the second sheet I saw when I flipped it said, “This is why you’re a dame oppai, you know?”—obviously an insult targeted at me.

Silently, I flipped the second sheet.

“There’s nothing more. Sorry to get your hopes up, okay?”

Ignoring the text on the third sheet, I looked on the back of the second, but nothing was written.

“Daaah!”

I screamed and tore the three sheets of paper to shreds.

Those searching looked over at me, wondering what had happened.

“It’s nothing at all!”

I apologized for startling them and took deep breaths to suppress my anger toward that guy.

Lowering my head, my eyes fell on a book; picking it up, I sat on his bedding and decided not to move. When I opened the book, a sheet of paper fell out.

What’s this? I picked up the paper and flipped it over; a long passage was written—it seemed to be a letter.

Instinctively, I checked the book’s title and found it was a novel called “Rock’s Adventure.”

What kind of letter could be stuck in such a novel?

(Could this be secret correspondence left by someone who used to work here? Or maybe some kind of code or an important hidden keyword!?)

My heart filled with hope as I read the letter I’d picked up.

“Hi, if you’re reading this, that means dame oppai has torn the board’s papers just as I intended and has this in hand. Unfortunately, this letter isn’t some kind of historical discovery you hoped for; it’s nothing of particular value, just something I wrote recently. However, if you read this to the end, I’ll grant you one piece of useful information. Put simply, it’s the way to open the hidden door.”

Reading up to here, I thought once again what an irritating lumpy bastard he was, but if that was the case, I’d read to the end, and I praised myself for holding back from tearing it up midway.

“The first thing you must do is admit to yourself that you have no value beyond your breasts. Next, strip to the waist right now and shout three times, ‘I am boobs.’ It’s preferable if there are people around. By doing this, I believe you’ll open the hidden door within yourself and walk a new life. Blessings upon the oppai who read to the end.”

I shredded the letter as I read, then stood and slammed the book I held to the ground.

“You rotten monster!”

I screamed with all my might, only to get smacked on the head by Lady Rusheru and scolded in turns by everyone until the search was over.

+++

## 83 - A Certain Elf’s Perspective (1)

In the magically lit underground—the dwelling of the new species of monster called Argos—I let out my umpteenth sigh of the day.

I knew the reason why.

“Why… is it always me…”

I sighed again, glancing at Ashiru, who sat in formal seiza posture with teary eyes, sniffling pitifully.

We had recently welcomed this girl into our clan, but I never imagined there could be someone so utterly incapable.

Now I understood that when Argos said, “She’s useless, so I’m giving her back,” he hadn’t been joking but serious.

That said, she was a precious link to him, and it would be a shame to let her go.

Above all, given that she had faced death twice because of Zesato’s tyranny, if I didn’t keep her under my protection, she could very well be killed.

It was true she couldn’t do housework or hunt.

Needless to say, she couldn’t farm, do physical labor, or handle paperwork properly, and with her poor aptitude for magic, any technical profession was out of the question.

If anything, she was clumsy and far from efficient, and even her motivation left much to be desired.

And yet, for some reason, she was overconfident.

From hearing her talk, I learned Ashiru had absolute confidence in her body and believed she’d someday seduce some convenient man and live securely for the rest of her life.

I thought, “Ah, so she aspires to be what’s called a femme fatale,” but it turned out she was still an innocent maiden who had never even dated a man.

I agonized over how to interpret this mismatch between words and actions.

In the end, I concluded, “Isn’t it simply that she has no will to try?” and decided to teach her the essentials for living.

The result, as I said earlier, was disastrous.

It was such a terrible state that everyone marveled, “How did she even survive this long?”

She wasn’t a bad girl—in fact, she was on the good-natured side—so abandoning her wasn’t an option, which only made things worse.

I wondered what kind of upbringing could produce such a girl and sent someone to inquire about her parents, only to learn something unbelievable.

“You see, that whole ‘If she tries, she can do it’ idea is an indulgent fantasy. She can’t do it even if she tries, and she knows it, so she has no motivation. About the only thing that girl can do is use her body to catch someone who’ll support her for life.”

Her own parents had already given up on her.

I thought, “Is that really okay?” but apparently the parents also had their calculations: “If she marries someone rich, we’ll have it easy too.” So they kept feeding her ideas to boost her confidence, and this was the result.

The Gardel clan’s reputation exceeded even what I had heard, and my knees nearly buckled.

However, I quickly recovered and decided to think positively.

Since I was in charge of looking after children, there could very well be more problem cases like her in the future.

Then she could be my practice run.

Determined to rehabilitate her no matter what, I took her into the clan and decided to give her thorough education.

But it was no use.

All she ever thought about was how to slack off, and even the basic training to get her to a functional level was apparently torture to her. At this rate, she’d probably run off before reaching the abilities of an ordinary person.

So I thought of finding her a marriage partner to prevent her from escaping, but—

“She’s just too big…”

“Combined with that lazy nature, it’s just plain vulgar.”

“Sorry, I prefer modest ones.”

The men’s opinions were harsh.

I myself felt guilty about foisting her on someone, so I shelved that idea.

It was right around when I was considering the next plan that she came with a proposal of her own.

Apparently she had thought things through in her own way and said, “I’ll accompany you when you make contact with Argos.”

She at least understood her position, and it was a request that neatly made use of the merit of having taken her in.

It turned out later that it was just an excuse to escape training.

Thus, upon arriving at the ruins, she became such a handful in the underground with her constant screeching that we had to calm her down to keep her from damaging the valuable Imperial materials.

Anyway, based on the information she provided, Argos had never gone deep into the underground, nor had he ever tried to stop her from entering, so we judged there was no problem with us proceeding inward.

According to Ashiru, there was some monster’s corpse deeper inside, and due to his massive size he couldn’t enter that part of the ruins, nor did he seem to care if people trespassed there.

“We can guess what the Empire was doing here… but judging by that girl’s terror, it was probably nothing good.”

I nodded at the words of the guard standing by my side.

After reporting the results of our preliminary search of the area, one of them approached with a troubled expression, holding something out.

“A book?”

“Yes, but… the contents are, how should I put it…”

She trailed off, but I couldn’t judge without seeing it.

So I took the book and flipped through the pages—and my mind went blank.

“Nude women… aren’t they?”

If this was in Argos’s dwelling, that would mean he read things like this.

I couldn’t process it, and when I checked other pages, they were mostly the same.

“Perhaps he just shoved any books he found into the shelf?”

Someone suggested, and everyone agreed.

As one of the men flipped through pages and expressed admiration while putting it back in its place, Ashiru, watching, commented:

“I’m definitely better-looking than that. I’d make a much better picture.”

As everyone looked on in exasperation, she added, “I’d like a job like that,” so I taught her some reality.

“I don’t know how they drew such detailed art, but to be a model you’d have to hold the same pose for hours, you know?”

It was an indirect way of saying, You couldn’t do that, but she didn’t seem to get it, responding, “Eh, all I have to do is sit still, right?” utterly clueless.

Having posed for a portrait before, I knew it was quite tough work and couldn’t imagine her managing it.

In any case, there was no point entertaining Ashiru further, so we all headed deeper into the ruins.

What we saw at the end of the dust-filled, sterile corridor lit by magic light exceeded our expectations.

“What… is this?”

Even he, who normally spoke carelessly, had a trembling voice and could barely keep composed.

Inside the chamber we reached lay a toppled cylindrical glass container. Inside it was something unmoving, as if asleep, something that could only be described as grotesque.

“That’s what I’d like to ask,” she replied.

I agreed with her.

There was no doubt it was something extraordinary.

However, no one suggested inspecting the inside, and we decided to check the other rooms.

From what we found, there were six monsters that appeared to be human-spider hybrids—or rather, as if forcibly stitched together.

And there were three other things truly deserving to be called “abominations,” different in shape from those inside the large transparent cylinders.

They were all definitely dead, but their appearances contrasted sharply.

Some looked as if they had suffered and writhed in agony until the moment of death, others looked peaceful, as if they had simply fallen asleep.

And there had been ten glass containers for the human-spider monsters.

“The numbers don’t match.”

At my murmur, several who had already noticed nodded solemnly.

“Lady Rusheru, how do you interpret this?”

“Most likely, an Imperial experimental facility. Experiments violating morality… I believe they died during the process. However—”

Yes, the numbers didn’t match.

There were nine corpses of experimental subjects, but ten containers.

“If one had simply been empty from the start, there’s no issue. But if a successful one left this place…”

“This is from two hundred years ago. We don’t need to be bound by the Empire’s past.”

“Even if one survived, that’s all ancient history,” someone said to dispel the unease.

Everyone agreed, and I nodded as well.

“An affront to life,” someone muttered, but I had nothing to say.

(The Empire has perished. But what they did and how they fell has not been accurately passed down.)

This wasn’t something I could judge.

Thinking that, I ordered them to gather the materials here.

But there weren’t even papers, let alone documents.

“We should have brought someone well-versed in Imperial affairs.”

No matter how we searched, we found nothing useful, and we couldn’t learn any details of the experiments here. Faced with this lack of information, I muttered my regrets.

On the other hand, I felt relieved, wondering what would have happened had we actually learned the full details of what had been here.

Whether bringing only fighters for Ashiru’s safety would turn out to be a blessing or a curse remained to be seen.

There were plenty of things better left unknown.

Even so, since we had seen it, we had no choice but to report it.

(Come to think of it… why did he bring her here?)

Did he not consider that someone who was guided to his dwelling could lead others here?

No, he likely anticipated it.

In that case, did he want us to see this?

(His massive body couldn’t fit through—but if he wanted someone to see it, he could have Ashiru do it… oh wait, she did see it already.)

At that moment, a phrase connected in my mind.

“Returning her because she’s useless”—was that what he meant?

I had already thought we needed to meet again.

That feeling only grew stronger, compelling me to act.

“Let’s leave a letter. Argos surely anticipated we’d come here… then it would be convenient for him to know we came.”

If he read the letter, surely he’d visit the elf village.

Who could have imagined exchanging letters with a monster?

There was no doubt about his intelligence—then what kind of message would pique his interest?

Feeling a little thrill, I walked back down the corridor.

A monster like none before, Argos; an Imperial experimental facility sleeping beneath the earth.

It might be improper, but I couldn’t help finding it fascinating.

Why did such dangerous fire-playing look so dazzling to someone like me?

+++

## 84

I ran across an empty plain.

My target lay to the east—specifically, the town of Gareia, located east-northeast of Brfu.

Rumor had it that a special individual called the “Emperor,” said to be equal to or even greater than me, was in that town now occupied by orcs with no humans left.

That said, the reliability of this information wasn’t high.

According to reports, this Emperor individual “moved around often,” and that town had simply been the last confirmed sighting.

According to acting commander Rachel Aunes, “It is currently the greatest threat to the Furon Council. If we eliminate it, our current forces should be able to push back the orc horde.”

In other words, its current combat evaluation was one army by itself—no different from my own self-evaluation.

I felt a little interested, but the words “it possesses terrifyingly durable armaments” killed my curiosity.

This great forest was former Imperial territory.

There was a chance it had simply gotten hold of some old Imperial equipment, so while the mystery hadn’t been revealed yet, there was likely to be an anticlimactic “just as I thought” ending. I kept my expectations moderate.

(Who would’ve thought the commander fled, an acting commander stepped up, and she turned out to be the spitting image of my sister…)

I wasn’t about to talk about fate, but when things lined up this neatly, it was a little unnerving.

For the record, my sister’s name was Mireina, and my younger sister’s was Serine.

The names weren’t identical, thankfully, but what if she turned out to be their relative and also a perfect look-alike?

As for the family name, apparently “after transitioning from Empire to Council, the nobility system was abolished, and all citizens began using family names.”

If I got the chance to visit again, I looked forward to bringing a magazine with my sister’s picture.

(Not good, I’m letting my mind wander. Thanks to the emotional suppression function slowly wearing off, my thoughts are getting a bit unstable.)

At any rate, if it was true that defeating this so-called “Emperor” meant the Furon Council could win, then taking it down meant she would be almost completely safe.

Thus, my current objective was first to find that Emperor.

I was headed toward Gareia, the site of the last sighting, but there had been no sign of orcs along the way yet.

I knew their horde was quite large, so they’d be easy enough to spot, but whether the target would be among them was the bothersome part.

And if I assumed that acting commander was “the girl of my family,” then there was a high chance she’d feed me false information just to use me.

Even if pressed later, she could just insist, “I believed that intel was accurate.” That much I could predict.

(She’d probably have no problem using me to reclaim the town… Well, I don’t mind being used once, but I expect compensation. Specifically, Imperial seasonings. Technologically, it shouldn’t be impossible, so they should exist.)

There were many other things I wanted, but first came food.

Improving my meals was still an unachieved goal.

If I couldn’t get them here, future improvements would be hopeless, so I could only hope.

If possible, I wanted a steady supply system, but that was up to them, so there was no point dwelling on it.

By now, I should’ve traveled far enough, but there was still no sign of the destination or enemies.

I decided to pick up the pace, since there weren’t even animals around for food.

By the time the sun began tilting, I finally drew close enough to spot the town.

(It’s not that big. Should I assume the low walls are why it fell, or was it the Emperor rampaging?)

If I stormed into town, how many residents would remain?

While I didn’t need to factor them in, rescuing them could help with future dealings with the Furon.

Like a side quest—I kept it in the corner of my mind as a “do it if possible” task, but I wouldn’t take unnecessary risks if the situation was dire.

There had been plenty I’d saved who ended up useless anyway, so I decided to limit my expectations and recklessness.

As I got closer, I could make out orcs atop the walls with my naked eyes. One of them was gnawing on what looked like a human arm.

As expected, the residents were being eaten.

That meant they’d either gotten bored playing with captives or broken them, so now they were food.

If they’d intended them for breeding, they’d have been sent to the rear immediately after the town’s capture, so there shouldn’t be any here.

Judging from the time since the town’s fall, I should probably assume there were no more survivors.

Since the flat plain offered no cover, the risk of being spotted was high, so I used my mimicry ability to approach.

Even an orc couldn’t detect me by scent at a distance.

Unspotted, I clung to the wall and deactivated the camouflage, climbing up.

Before the orc who spotted me could cry out, I chopped its neck with a hand strike, breaking its spine, and just as I was about to descend, I was noticed.

A deflated horn sound, “puoooon,” rang out, the player apparently incompetent, tilting his head and tooting.

I jumped down toward him to stop it.

Another orc, fed up, smacked the horn-blower, who then sounded a proper “BUOOO” alarm just as I landed before them.

I grabbed the terrified pig looking up at me and smashed it against the wall to kill it.

(Now, how should I proceed from here?)

Armed orcs would surely gather, and judging by the ruined townscape, the terrain advantage was theirs.

Even if I ceded the lay of the land, it wasn’t a bad idea to wreck the place first while looking for the Emperor.

If I caused a scene, there was a chance it’d come to me, and either way, the pigs were destined for slaughter.

Trudging along past rubble, I stepped onto the main street and spotted a group of armed orcs.

(First that group. Then I’ll run toward the center.)

By the time I had the thought, I’d already trampled the orc group.

Realizing they couldn’t stop me, the orcs dropped their shields and braced their spears in a hedge formation.

I decided to teach them that switching from one hand to two hands was pointless; instead of charging, I grabbed a huge chunk of rubble and hurled it at the spear formation.

A stationary target made for an easy hit.

(Throwing big rubble is nice. I can wipe out groups and keep my hands clean.)

A new addition to my limited ranged attacks repertoire.

With that, I began collecting large, heavy objects around me to practice throwing accurately, strolling down the main street.

As I tossed gifts at each orc group that appeared and made my way toward the center, I spotted a clearly larger pig ahead.

(Not the Emperor… just an armed King. Just an oversized regular orc.)

If one of those was around, then there was likely no Emperor here.

Disappointed, I slumped my shoulders and swept aside the approaching pig with the wooden pillar I carried.

It felt comfortable enough that I kept it in hand as I headed straight for the King.

Apparently offended by my nonchalance, it pointed its greatsword at me and started snorting loudly, barking out orders.

No need to guess; the heavily armed orcs stepped up to block my way.

The lesser orcs cleared the path, and as I reached the central plaza, the pigs instantly filled the main street behind me.

They thought they’d cut off my retreat, but they didn’t understand I could shatter their lines with a simple charge.

In any case, I stepped into the plaza where the orcs had gathered. As I advanced, the frontmost heavily armored orc held out an empty hand in a stop gesture.

I had no obligation to listen, but there was no harm in finding out what they wanted.

If anything, I was a little curious what they’d try in this situation.

Once they confirmed I’d stopped, one of the eight pigs glanced back and gave a big nod.

The King climbed a pile of rubble, raised its greatsword to the sky, and snorted noisily.

After a while of raucous squealing, it pointed the sword at me and started “gup-gup” laughing; the others joined in with laughter.

(I see. They want to make an example of me—or maybe turn me into a spectacle, a new toy.)

Whether my guess was right or not, the orc king suddenly cut off its laughter and jerked its chin to signal the armored orcs.

Eight of them stepped forward.

So I stepped forward too.

The distance between both sides closed, and since we both carried large weapons, our reach overlapped quickly.

“PUGUUUOOO!”

The King roared, a shout to start the match.

So I hurled the pillar I was holding straight at the pig king.

+++

## 85

The pillar I hurled flew straight toward the Orc King.

It managed to react instinctively and block with its greatsword, but before the pillar’s sheer mass there was no bracing; it tumbled down from the pile of rubble.

Satisfied that things had gone exactly as planned, I nodded, while the eight heavily armored orcs glanced back, faces pale.

(They’re probably thinking, “Win or lose, we’re screwed,” but a victory for you lot isn’t even on the table.)

So, to make victory even more certain, I mercilessly enacted my next move.

Dropping my pack, I charged into the mob of fodder orcs that had blocked off the street, as if to say, “Heeeyah! Time for a brawl!”

(Feels like I’m in an “Ore VS” game!)

I’m talking about those games where a single player takes on overwhelming hordes in hack-and-slash fashion, enemies throwing sheer numbers at you—the “Ore VS Series.” In this case, it’d be “Ore VS Orcs.”

For the record, my favorite was the third game, Ore VS Aliens.

Being able to snatch weapons from enemies and fly into the sky for a one-man army rampage was the highlight; a far cry from the first game where you just ran around mowing down goblin-level fodder.

Humming the series’ trademark “victory is assured” execution BGM in my head, I indulged in an all-out massacre on the main street jam-packed with pigs.

I ignored the heavily armored orcs bellowing like, “We came to fight you!” and instead slaughtered the panicked pigs fleeing all around, as if to say, “I didn’t come to fight you. I came to kill you.”

Punch to kill.

Kick to kill.

Throw to kill.

The unarmored orcs were fragile to a ridiculous degree.

Their meat wall crumbled instantly, and the main street became flooded with pigs trampling each other to flee.

I lobbed huge chunks of rubble into the throng to sow confusion and expand the casualties.

This worked perfectly; fallen orcs were trampled to death by those behind them.

The heavily armored ones tried running clumsily to stop me, but naturally, the more armor, the slower you moved.

Even at their best, orc speed was nothing to brag about; there was no way they could keep up with me darting all over the slaughterhouse.

Perhaps realizing this after running themselves ragged, they resorted to banging weapons against shields and waving while “boo-boo” squealing in an attempt to provoke me.

Naturally, I ignored them.

There were still plenty of pigs needing slaughter; I had no time to waste on fools.

The main street was overflowing with pig corpses, and the Orc King back atop the rubble was throwing a tantrum, shrieking furiously.

Seems the fall earlier knocked it out cold; its painfully late revival was earning it pitiful looks from the other orcs.

But the return of their commander dramatically changed the battle.

The warrior-type orcs gathered in one spot at its command.

I dove right in and quickly butchered them all—most efficient slaughter method ever.

As for the heavily armored orcs still panting, I planned to save them for last.

Having learned that a commander made the armed orcs easier to dispatch en masse, I waited for its next command—but apparently, the pigs learned too, ignoring orders and fleeing.

What a lack of popularity… or pigularity.

“Useless pigs like this are no longer needed,” I thought, and leaped at the Orc King, intent on mercilessly snapping its neck—but surprisingly, it dodged my aerial strike with a nimble backstep.

Not only that, it stepped in heavily to match my landing and swung down its greatsword.

Naturally, such an oversized swing would never connect; instead, I stomped on the greatsword with my foot—yet it didn’t break.

(Huh, did I hold back too much?)

Still, the impact felt strange, so I kicked its arm with my other leg, pried the greatsword from its remaining hand, and tapped its material with my fist to test it.

(Yeah, that’s crazy hard. And light for its size.)

Up close, I could also tell the blade was crudely made, like someone just shaved down a metal plate.

In other words, this greatsword was likely crafted by grinding down Imperial alloy.

This cast doubt on what exactly this so-called Emperor was.

At the same time, one concern arose.

If they truly were armed with Imperial-made alloy, then musket fire wouldn’t penetrate.

If I assumed this thing was “granted a portion of it,” then for the Furon Council-State to win against the orc horde, they’d need to confiscate these alloy weapons.

This could turn into a bigger hassle than expected, I thought with a heavy sigh.

As an aside, I hacked the begging, squealing one-armed pig with the stolen greatsword.

A little small, but usable like a short sword.

Of course, since it wasn’t originally designed as a weapon, its sharpness was nothing to boast of.

Still, it was a weapon meant to be swung with brute strength; I’d need to handle it more gently, or it’d get scrapped if I used it seriously.

All the same, I’d picked up a usable weapon.

Trudging toward the panicked heavily armored orcs, the eight pigs began quarreling as if saying, “You go! No, you go!”

Realizing they couldn’t escape, they were probably trying to pick a sacrificial lamb to buy time—but how many seconds could that really buy?

And even if they stalled me for ten seconds, what would that accomplish?

In the end, the orcs failed to pick a first victim even as I loomed before them; all eight departed to the afterlife together in harmony.

The alloy sword’s blade got blunted in the process, effectively class-changing into a club.

After that, I just needed to chase down the stragglers; my work here was done. I hurried back to sling on my pack.

As I hurled my new blunt weapon at fleeing pigs for sport and chased them, the sun had completely set. I searched the town for any survivors.

After disposing of about twenty orcs trying to hide and wait things out, my ears picked up a curious sound.

I paused to listen, and the sound also stopped.

(Hmm… that’s a human voice, right? And it doesn’t sound desperate… like they were chatting?)

No way anyone could’ve survived hidden in an orc-occupied town for days.

(Then they must’ve arrived recently… or there’s a hidden passage—oh, the sewers!)

Based on the direction and circumstances, that was likely correct.

If there were Imperial survivors, they’d at least have built sewers.

But my massive body couldn’t fit inside.

I had no choice but to ignore whoever might be below and give up on the intel they likely had.

I resigned myself, but it really was inconvenient not being able to speak in situations like this.

Anyway, there was still much to do, so I forced myself to drop the matter.

Judging that I’d cleared out most of the orcs hiding in town, I next pursued those that escaped.

Enough time had passed that they’d feel safe and flee straight back to their boss.

So I left town, tracking the pigs by their footprints.

Plenty had fled; finding them wasn’t hard.

The problem was that some panicked pigs might’ve fled in random directions.

I prioritized checking south where I thought the front line was, but barely any tracks led that way.

Looking east, I found plenty of tracks, all heading north.

Those that fled through the north gate also left tracks heading north.

(This is practically confirmed, huh?)

First, follow the tracks.

Whether the special individual “Emperor” was at the end, I couldn’t say, but at minimum there should be clues.

Now then, let’s corner the pigs.

I’d keep hunting them until they remembered that they were the ones meant to be hunted.

+++

## 86

It had grown completely dark, and I stood alone amid scattered orc corpses.

Tracking them had gone fine, but once those pigs—who thought they’d gotten away—started shambling and eventually stopped moving altogether, my patience snapped, and this mess was the result.

They had probably decided they’d be killed if they reported back, so they chose self-preservation and never returned.

Which meant I had no choice but to search for another group’s footprints. Even with good night vision, it was still hard to see in this darkness.

“Makes things real troublesome.”

I snarled, taking it out on a nearby pig.

With the last survivor finished, I reluctantly began searching the darkened plain.

Soon after, I found another group’s tracks more easily than expected.

So my sense of direction had been right after all.

(Well, I don’t know how long this will take, but it doesn’t look like my face-to-face with the orc boss is that far off.)

Come to think of it, I still hadn’t met an opponent who could let me fully unleash this body’s specs.

Spider-man was a terrible match-up; that was closer to a struggle for survival than a fight.

We were out to kill each other, but thanks to how slowly he switched gears, I easily snatched victory.

Fighting humans was just playtime—hardly worth mentioning.

When I faced that strangely large Shadow Viper, I got to flex my strength to my heart’s content, but it was far from what you’d call a battle—just overpowering with brute force.

The Red Ogre had been close to satisfying.

Its raw strength wasn’t bad, but everything else was.

Ever since I got this body and learned what it could do, a desire occasionally poked through—to “enjoy all-out combat.”

That wish still hadn’t been granted, and even now, though I hoped “this time for sure,” I couldn’t expect much when there was the concern that their true nature might just be “orcs armed with Imperial alloy gear.”

In any case, the plan to offer up the cocky orcs and their ringleader as a blood sacrifice hadn’t changed, and I had no complaints about tracking them through the night.

Not sleeping for a day or two wasn’t a problem, and not needing to eat was, once again, incredibly convenient.

After following the footprints for a while, a large structure came into view.

(That’s… a stadium? Pretty collapsed, but it still keeps enough of the original shape to tell. So this is their base.)

There should only be three stadiums within the Empire, so I now knew roughly where I was.

The problem was that I was unfamiliar with southern Imperial geography, so I couldn’t count on knowing the state of nearby towns.

The terrain around here had little relief, and it felt a bit strange that I couldn’t see any remains of towns, but I had no way of knowing what had happened in the last two hundred years.

Even so, I couldn’t abide their using Imperial architecture.

Sure, with some of the roof still intact, this would make a perfectly adequate stronghold.

The interior passages were built for humans, so orcs could use them too, and if usable fixtures remained, it would be better than building something shoddy from scratch.

But it grated on me that mere pigs would make use of the Empire’s legacy.

Judging this, like with the goblins, to require extermination, I approached the stadium and used my telescopic vision to look for an infiltration route.

Bonfires burned near the entrances, and several orcs seemed to be patrolling as lookouts.

I also needed to think about where to stash my gear. Getting in would be easy, but I wanted a spot that suited my purposes.

So I kept just outside the lookouts’ sight range and circled the stadium all the way around.

In the end, I decided that slipping in through a collapsed section of the roof was optimal—there was even a spot where I could safely leave my pack.

I sprinted for the stadium, leapt onto the wall, and power-climbed up—and, as was only natural, I was spotted immediately.

There was no helping it; stealth against orcs only went so far.

At the roof section I’d found, I hung my backpack where it would catch and also be concealed, then jumped down about six meters and made a dynamic landing in the stands.

At that, I felt every orc in the stadium turn to look at me at once.
