Even if I planned to search for the Emperor, it would be far less effort to draw it out than to find it among what had to be thousands of orcs.

“GRAAAAAAAAAAH!”

So I roared.

With the forceful declaration of “I’m right here,” I swaggered through enemy territory as if I owned the place, provoking the local boss to come out.

But I got no reaction, and the surrounding orcs showed no sign of charging.

With no one moving to stop me, I kept walking and ended up down on the field.

Still no response—did that mean the boss here was a coward?

Thinking that, I heard some kind of cry reach my ears.

The voice wasn’t loud.

But in contrast, the orcs moved quickly.

The wall of bodies—no, the wall of pigs—split, and a straight path opened.

To that silent “Proceed this way,” I snorted in answer.

There was no doubt the boss was up ahead, and from the orcs’ movements, their discipline looked high.

Even so, I figured one of those who had fled from me earlier had to be here too, and from the look of things, they hadn’t managed to overwrite their fear.

In other words, they feared the boss more than me and judged me the weaker.

As I walked the pig road, observing my surroundings, it slowly appeared.

Ahead of my gaze—I saw a monster that looked like an armed orc climbing up onto a dais.

(Looks orc-ish… but also not.)

A special specimen that made me nod “I see.” It definitely looked orc-like, but something was off.

Because it was armored, I couldn’t see everything, but the face was close to an orc’s while the build leaned ogre—an unfamiliar, taut physique that made me tilt my head.

As for its equipment, it carried a ridiculously huge saber and a plain, undecorated slab of a tower shield, and its helmet and armor were probably leather. To face me, that seemed perfectly reasonable.

But with a build closer to an ogre and a body this ripped—so unlike an orc—“Emperor” felt less fitting than something like “Muscle Orc.”

(So the name must come from its command ability? Strong as an individual, but it shows its true value in the horde. In that case, let’s see how far it can push back against me—someone who has honed individual strength to the limit.)

I strode forward at my leisure, while the Emperor waited atop the dais.

When our distance shrank under twenty meters, the Emperor raised the saber in its hand.

“OOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOOAAAH!!”

A true war cry.

This was what you called a manly roar.

The pigs encircling us all at once sprinted up into the stands, while the orcs who remained hurried to set up braziers, lit them, and ran off at a jog.

Light filled the stadium, and the stage was set.

The Emperor leapt down from the dais and landed with a thud that made the ground resound, then started walking toward me.

Our distance fell under ten meters, and we faced each other in a natural stance.

No signal was needed—this fight had no rules, and its only decision would be the other’s death.

I lowered my center of gravity, and in response the Emperor raised its shield.

(Good. First, a test of strength.)

Seems this one “gets it,” I almost smiled—but I didn’t hold back.

I kicked off the ground and charged the set shield.

I wasn’t going all out yet, but at a speed that would absolutely blast a normal ogre away, my mass slammed in.

With a great bang, the Emperor slid back several meters while taking my charge on the tower shield, yet it held its posture and dug in.

As for me, I hadn’t expected to be stopped. I was shocked, but immediately stepped in again.

But it didn’t move—no, I couldn’t move it.

(No way… are we evenly matched!?)

Surprise after surprise.

Just as I thought I’d show it my true power, a saber thrust shot out from beside the shield.

I had no intention of taking that head-on, so I side-stepped to evade and fired a left straight.

Of course it was blocked by the shield, but a hardness beyond expectation ran up my fist.

However, the next strike wouldn’t be so gentle.

Much as I wanted to enjoy this longer, being held off like that was irritating.

So I drove a full-power right straight into the tower shield—not at the body, but aiming to break the equipment.

My punch struck true, and a thunderous boom echoed through the stadium.

The feel in my hand—was nothing.

The Emperor staggered back a few steps, but while a faint imprint of my fist remained, the shield was otherwise intact.

(Hey, hold on a second.)

I knew that feeling.

It was the same as back then.

Right after I woke… that moment of despair.

(That’s the same metal they used for the Gate! What the hell kind of gear are you wearing!)

If it was the Gate’s metal, then even at a tower shield’s thickness, it could probably stop my full power.

“Annoying thing!” I snarled—and at the same time, I understood perfectly why the Furon Council-State had been helpless.

And the Emperor’s own power was close to matching mine.

I felt keenly that this was too heavy a burden for humans.

So why was I enjoying this so much?

I knew why—because the thing before my eyes could rightly be recognized as an “enemy,” a genuine threat.

I looked up at the night sky to cool my excitement and let out a long breath.

I shifted my gaze back to the Emperor, sank my hips, and set both hands to the ground.

All right—let’s begin the fight.

+++

## 87

The time spent waiting for an opening was often drawn out in manga.

But in reality, it could end in a flash.

Part of it was my lack of patience, but since my opponent had taken a defensive stance, it was inevitable that things turned out this way.

So I took the initiative, but the way it parried my heavy blows and firmly absorbed my flurries was impressive—no half-baked creature could manage that kind of skill.

Even so, those exchanges weren’t meaningless.

Because after leaping at the Emperor and giving it no chance to breathe under my barrage, I discovered one of its strengths.

(A terrifying reaction speed—this thing is definitely moving after it sees my attacks.)

It felt like the absurdity of a fighting game: “Saw the light jab, easy invincible reversal.”

I had confirmed that my power and speed were superior, but it compensated with reaction speed, keeping itself safe while slipping in counters just enough to stop me from pressing through.

My fists now carried slight cuts, proof that saber really could injure me.

If I took a proper slash, it would be dangerous; I never thought I’d be forced to dodge in a straight fight.

(Heh, a pig with a weapon versus a monster without one… at this rate, who can tell which of us was once human?)

I laughed inwardly and relished the fight.

After the strength contest, I tried feints and tactics, but they were all meaningless against that reaction speed.

When I rushed in, it coolly slipped sabers in at the right moments to interrupt me.

It sounded like a dead end, but I had one more advantage.

Stamina. More than anything, the Emperor’s saber and shield were huge and heavy.

If it kept fighting while carrying that weight, it was obvious which one of us would tire first.

No matter how much I pressed the attack, I had the stamina to run for a whole day, so this wasn’t a problem.

Even if I went all out, the more I did, the more its endurance would wear down, bringing the end faster.

I hadn’t landed a decisive blow yet, but the situation was in my favor.

So I didn’t let up.

Perhaps sensing it too, the Emperor started to show impatience.

Specifically, it tried to swap offense and defense, repeatedly attempting to change position.

(Of course I won’t allow that! Now then—will you bow to my power and speed, or will you collapse from exhaustion! I’ll at least let you choose!)

I kept up the assault, not even allowing a reset, until finally, the Emperor lashed out with an attack meant for mutual destruction.

I saw it as my chance.

Its strike cut across my chest, but my right hook slipped past the shield and smashed into its head.

A red line was carved across my chest, blood trickling down my stomach—but the wound was shallow. I traced it with my thumb, then licked off the blood.

The bleeding stopped quickly, and the wound would soon close.

Showing that off, I laughed.

This was the final piece—my regeneration narrowed its options even further.

I pressed the attack again.

Before the head damage could clear, I would finish it.

Even while wary of the saber, I ground away at its stamina through the shield.

Defense might prevent direct wounds, but eventually the arms holding that shield would reach their limit.

And if it came forward willing to trade, regeneration made it my win.

If it stayed on the defensive, exhaustion made it my win.

(So, what will you do!? Show me your answer, ruler of the orcs!)

Since I outsped it, countering was its best option for landing a fatal blow.

Naturally I knew that, and it knew I knew, so neither of us rushed into a reckless gamble.

That’s why my trick worked perfectly.

While fighting to always stay close, I closed the gap further—and the moment the Emperor went to attack, my tail snagged its left leg from the shield’s blind spot.

I meant to topple it and strike, but it jammed the tower shield into the ground, forcibly righting itself while thrusting the saber.

With its aim at my neck, I couldn’t press in, so I dodged and chopped at its outstretched arm, but my awkward position kept it from being effective.

Even so, I wasn’t letting it reset. The instant it pulled the saber back, I kicked the tower shield it had braced in the ground.

Knocked back slightly, it couldn’t slash at my follow-through leg. Landing with a bitter grimace, it was hit again by my relentless blows.

The Emperor retreated step by step; for every step it lost, I pressed forward, maintaining my range, refusing to let it shake me off, my full physical ability clinging tight.

(If I’ve pushed this far, it can’t keep holding back. Soon, it’ll come at me with everything.)

I pressed without pause, forcing it into a position where it had no choice but to act.

To win after drawing out all it had—that was how much this foe was worth.

(Every fight makes me stronger. Sorry, but with this monster’s body of mine, I’ll make you my sustenance.)

It was the choice only a worthy foe could inspire.

I already saw my path to victory, but if it overturned it, I’d simply surpass that too.

If a fine meal was transformed into the finest, all the better.

Even now, while withstanding my onslaught, it sought a way to strike back—proving it still believed it had a chance.

I couldn’t help but look forward: “What will you try next?”

The longer it dragged on, the worse for it—so it had to make a move soon. And as I guessed, during a flurry softened by my own expectations, it acted.

The tower shield flicked my arm upward.

At the same time, it slashed from behind the blind. The target was my leg.

A red line was carved into my ankle, but unfortunately for it, that saber’s reach made that the limit.

It wasn’t enough to slow me. On the contrary, the price of that one strike was that my fist hammered into the tower shield, breaking its stance, and my follow-up roundhouse blasted its body through the shield.

But maybe that was its plan—reducing the damage by flying back itself, then regaining distance to reset.

Now I had to find a way past that saber to close in again.

Which meant, in terms of maneuvering, I’d lost.

Trading minor damage for that gain was more than worth it.

(Very rational! For a monster, that’s an awfully rational decision! Is it experience? Or does it just have that much combat sense?)

I nearly burst out laughing with delight.

The wound on my leg would close soon enough, so I let it recover too.

Drawing this out to gain more experience wasn’t bad—in fact, it was best.

I decided not to rush the finish, and to hide that decision, I circled at a steady distance as if raising my guard.

But perhaps sensing it couldn’t win by defense, it suddenly hurled aside the shield. Holding the saber in both hands, it shifted into a quiet, flowing movement like a martial dance, blade swinging as it dropped into a stance.

That was not a motion a monster should take.

It was unmistakably martial arts—and I knew that stance.

(…Wait. Why are you using that stance? No, that can’t be! Unless—)

I looked around, found what I sought, and ignored my enemy to move there.

I snatched up one of the poles holding a brazier, spilling fire onto the ground to light it, then used the pole to write words large enough for it to see.

“Teiha Bujin-ryū”

That flowing series of motions it had taken on—those came from a video parody based on a famous manga in the Empire.

It started as a “Since I had the chance, I made it practical” joke series. But eventually the real dojo that inspired the manga cooperated, and the videos continued until it had become truly practical.

Having watched in real time, I couldn’t mistake it.

Every male student had probably mimicked that stance once—and now it was recreated before me.

There was no doubt. This was the same as me—a Genetically Enhanced Soldier.

Maybe realizing that I understood, it froze in that stance, staring blankly at the words I had written.

But that didn’t end the fight.

“GUAAAAAHAHAHAHAHAHA!”

That laugh was madness—its bloodshot eyes glared, its saber-wielding steps pounded forward with nothing but hostility.

(So… you hate the Empire, huh?)

That much I could understand.

I accepted that the battle couldn’t be avoided.

But I was wrong.

The clear killing intent it turned on me—I didn’t understand what it meant.

Its massive body strode straight at me, grinning with madness, then pounced as if it couldn’t restrain itself anymore.

Its style was nothing like before, and I reflexively leapt back.

With hands planted on the ground, it looked up at me with bloodshot eyes, drool dripping unchecked from its mouth.

What had driven it this far into madness?

Was there something I couldn’t comprehend?

As I stepped back from its ferocity, it laughed and shouted:

“Yoh, yag! Mizgueda! Zan, ninmeh!”

+++

## 88

It was unconscious.

Before I realized it, I had taken a step back.

Was I intimidated?

No—the sudden change had been so grotesque that I instinctively raised my alert level.

But more than that, I felt fear from the face twisted with mad delight.

(He did say “found you.” That means he was searching for a comrade.)

There could be countless reasons, but why would he look at me with those eyes?

If he glared at me as though I were a mortal enemy, I could understand.

Even if we were both Genetically Enhanced Soldiers, hating me as a remnant of the Empire—the one that turned him into a monster—would make sense.

I myself had been nearly tricked into it, and not everyone could accept it as willingly as I did.

No matter how I considered every possibility, I couldn’t understand this situation.

So was this a mismatch of perception caused by my lack of information?

(Maybe something was planted in him before he became a Genetically Enhanced Soldier… or maybe he learned something he shouldn’t have?)

It was entirely possible.

If so, the only choice was to beat it out of him.

I had to first knock him down, then have the proper conversation.

With that conclusion—“hit him until he calms down”—I squared off against my crazed comrade, his bloodshot eyes bulging as though he might spring at me any second.

My reaction wasn’t slow.

But his charge came at a speed unlike before, and I failed to dodge the slash that followed—a painful mistake.

A deeper gash than ever before tore into my left arm, blood dripping red down as I clicked my tongue.

(Just throwing away the shield couldn’t make him this fast… No, that’s it!)

If he was like me, a Genetically Enhanced Soldier, it wouldn’t be strange for him to have a special ability.

And if he hadn’t used it until now, it must have some restriction—or a price.

(Time limit, or penalty afterward… damn, that’s the kind of manga-like power I wish I had too!)

Twice, three times, he swung. I barely dodged, but stalling wasn’t an option.

I needed to go on the offensive somehow, but if I got careless against a saber that could cut my hide, I’d bleed badly.

Even if I looked for an opening, his reaction speed would counter it.

“Damn, I can’t find a way to attack,” I muttered as I dodged slashes, then tried to strike with my tail—only to have its tip sliced off.

It wasn’t even a centimeter, so no pain and no handicap, but it reminded me how impossible his reflexes were to crack.

Focusing completely on evasion, the roles had reversed when I spotted something.

It was the discarded tower shield. That saber was likely forged from the same alloy, but with the shield’s thickness, it wouldn’t cut through.

I grinned wide, leapt back, and as he closed in, I kicked up dirt to blind him.

He guarded his eyes, but a fair bit of dirt got into his mouth, making him stop and spit.

I took the chance to sprint full speed to the shield, grabbed it, and turned back with a smug grin, holding it up.

(Not bad… in fact, I think I’ll keep this.)

After all, equipment my size was always hard to come by.

Taking it as compensation after this “conversation” wouldn’t be unreasonable.

Burying him and taking both shield and saber crossed my mind too, but whether that happened depended on him. In his crazed state, though, I doubted a real dialogue was possible.

Still, I had to try.

“AAAAAAAHHHH!”

He roared at the sight of me bracing behind the shield.

(Too late to regret not tossing it far away. Since I picked it up, I’ll make full use of it.)

He leapt with a strong attack; I stepped back to evade, then blocked the follow-up combo of quick, quick, strong slashes with the shield.

As expected, the tower shield held easily.

I absorbed the blows and watched for a chance to strike back.

(Whatever conditions his ability requires, this furious assault shows he can’t keep it up long!)

As I kept blocking, he changed approach.

He pressed the saber against the shield, trying to brute-force it.

“That’s a mistake,” I thought—but no, boosted as he was, I couldn’t overpower it even with all my strength.

The increase was huge. But the strain on his body had to be as well. If he had started this way, I would have been forced to retreat.

Sensing that brute force wouldn’t decide it, he switched the saber to one hand, reaching with the other for my shield.

He meant to wrench it open and thrust inside—but that was far too hasty.

As expected, he used his enhanced reflexes to knock my shield aside, then thrust straight for my heart.

But I’d anticipated it.

I arched back, falling away as I kicked his arm.

The saber spun up into the air, and as he looked up, I tackled him, driving him down.

He tried to kick me off, but weight was on my side.

We fought bare-fisted for control, both hammering away unguarded.

Then I caught it—his eyes darting away for an instant.

Grabbing both my arms, he pulled me in, planted his feet in my stomach, and threw me with his whole body. He rolled, stood, and ran.

I knew where—toward the saber stuck in the ground.

I was ready.

The moment he bolted, I snatched the shield again and chased.

When he grabbed the sword, I slammed into him with a shield bash aimed at his sword arm, then gripped the shield with both hands and battered him like a bludgeon.

Even with fast reflexes, once his stance was broken, he couldn’t answer cleanly.

That’s why I poured into a storm of shield strikes.

His arm went numb, and at last the saber slipped free. I kicked it away, then hurled the shield at it, sending the weapon spinning even farther out.

(Here we go—the hand-to-hand brawl we’ve been waiting for!)

I stepped in and smashed a heavy punch into his gut.

The leather armor dulled it, but the damage still landed.

With both of us bare-handed, my superior durability made this the surest path to victory.

He knew it too, but I didn’t give him a chance to retrieve a weapon.

Forced into melee, the damage stacked on his body.

And then, even as my fists pounded him, he caught one arm in both hands and sank his teeth into it.

On reflex, I hammered his face and pried him off, but blood ran from the bite marks.

(So you’ll fight dirty? Fine, then…!)

With that bleeding arm, I struck his nose with a light, fast jab.

Catching his flinch, I stomped his foot, then drove my fist deep into his side.

His arm clamped around my neck, but I answered with a body blow.

When he let go, I slammed a straight punch into his face, knocking him stumbling back several steps but not down.

At this point, the difference was clear.

And yet his face flushed red, veins bulging all over his body.

No doubt it was the cost of his boost.

I thought the fight was decided. But he didn’t.

He staggered toward me, arm outstretched.

“HAYAGU, GWAZERO!”

That undefended hand—those unarmored fingers—I seized the chance.

I bit down hard and tore them off.

He stared blankly at his hand, and as I spat the four severed fingers, for some reason, I found myself chewing and swallowing the scraps of flesh clinging to the bone.

+++

## 89

It wasn’t on impulse; the finger meat slid down my throat as naturally as breathing.

I only snapped back to myself once I was crunching the remaining bones to bits.

(No—wait. This makes no sense. Trying to spit it out yet chewing and swallowing isn’t a simple “mistake.”)

Against my will I crushed the bones in my mouth, and the instant the scraps of meat brushed my tongue, a shock ran through me hard enough to steal my awareness.

What flavor—did something this delicious truly exist in this world?

What had all the things I’d thought “tasty” up to now even been?

Even while intoxicated, the calm part of me judged this an impossible line of thought, but I didn’t know how long that calm would last.

The man before me was the same as I was—a Genetically Enhanced Soldier, a former Imperial citizen, a compatriot.

(To regard that as “choice meat”…)

As my thoughts reeled, the words he’d spoken flashed through my head.

“Hurry… feed me.”

What did that mean?

“You’re… the third.”

So you’ve already eaten two?

“Finally found you.”

Did the search itself drive him mad like this?

(No—that feeling is exactly like back then—)

My brain refused to accept it.

But I admitted it.

A joy that swelled the more I ate.

A euphoria that could steal even my consciousness.

Driven by a senseless “must eat,” I lunged and tore in, riding the impulse.

(I remember now. I’ve already eaten one.)

Only now did I finally understand.

That time in the forest, the enormous octopus-thing I’d attacked, thinking it was an unknown monster—it was likely a Genetically Enhanced Soldier like me. And what I felt biting into its flesh was happening again now.

The compatriot before my eyes looked irresistibly alluring—and he surely saw me the same way.

I clamped a hand over my mouth, battling the urge to pounce this instant.

But no matter how I resisted, he—already swallowed by the impulse—was different.

As if saying “To hell with odds,” he staggered toward me on unsteady feet despite clearly being in an abnormal state.

When his maimed hand touched me and he opened his mouth to grin in delight, it was obvious he was already beyond saving.

“Ya… d—guel…”

Even pity didn’t move me to say “I’ll end this for you.”

His voice, the smell of blood, the meat visible in the wound—all of it unhinged me.

He tottered closer, mouth opening to bite.

With only a sliver of distance left, my reason burst.

“GRAAAAAAAAAH!”

I thrust out both hands, set them on his head and shoulder, and bit into the side of his neck through the leather armor.

A scream reached me.

I spat out the chunk of flesh I’d torn free, ripped the leather, then bit off his upper arm as it flailed in the air.

Chewing that meat, trembling at the flavor, I shoved the feast before me to the ground and devoured him.

His resistance meant nothing. As my vision ran red with blood, I thought I heard a small “Help me.”

That became my last memory of the fight.

I opened my eyes.

I wouldn’t hide behind “I blacked out.”

He was someone I killed—someone I ate.

Before me lay a ravaged corpse with no shape left, and no trace of orcs remained in the stadium.

Lit by the braziers, the remains gleamed so red they made even the clots of dark blood glow, forcing me to grasp how outrageously much meat I had packed into my gut.

I wiped my blood-smeared mouth with my arm, but with my whole body drenched in blood, it made no difference anywhere.

(I have to move from here…)

If it was like before, an irresistible drowsiness would hit me after this.

I needed to reach a safe place before I fell asleep.

But I had no idea where such a place might be, and a spike of panic rose.

I had to retrieve the gear I’d stashed, but now wasn’t the time.

(Right… then I should just destroy the stadium entrances so the orcs can’t get in, shouldn’t I?)

Rather than search for a place that might not exist, it was better to turn this into a safe zone.

Sure, something like me could still get in, but even so, I could at least avoid the worst.

I sprinted up into the stands and destroyed an exit.

“How many more are there?” Impatience needled me, but the demolition went smoothly, and I could move quickly between them.

The braziers on the field didn’t give enough light. It was hard to see, but my night vision made it manageable.

Then it was only a matter of time.

As before, it became a race against sleep, and after smashing the third exit, drowsiness came on at once.

But I still had a little time.

I rushed to destroy the remaining exits.

Whether I made it in time, I wouldn’t know until I woke up.

(I’ve done what I can. Now it’s up to luck…)

In the empty stands, I sat, crushing a seat too small for me, and closed my eyes without resisting the sleep that crashed over me.

Light—whether it was sunrise, I couldn’t tell yet, but I did wake safely.

No—“ended up waking” was the truer feeling.

How long I’d slept wasn’t worth thinking about.

I stood, looked around, found what I wanted, and walked toward it.

At an unremarkable concrete block, I twisted my body and drew back my arm.

“Damn it aaaaaaaaaaall!”

I slammed a full-power blow into the block and pulverized it.

I touched my throat to confirm the voice that had just come out, but I felt not the slightest joy.

Without a word, I threw another punch. The concrete shattered, opening a side hole in the ruined entrance.

Searching for somewhere to vent my anger, I looked for the next target.

The scientist’s laughter from my dream still rang in my ears. My emotional switch flipped again and again, but the rage didn’t stop.

Twice, three times, I smashed the wall. When the emotion-suppression function finally kicked in hard enough to give me a brutal headache, my head cooled enough to stay level.

“…What good is getting my voice back now?”

Sitting on a step in the stands, I covered my face with hands caked in dried blood.

I thought about what came next.

But no matter how I tried to think forward, I couldn’t laugh at the future waiting for me.

With my feelings a mess, I couldn’t think straight, and the remains I glimpsed through my fingers dragged me back to reality.

“It must’ve been hell for you… It must’ve hurt so much…”

Thinking back, that giant octopus I ate might have reached that state at the end of the same torment.

“So it was ‘one more’ left for you… No wonder you kept coming, even with no chance to win…”

And now it was my turn to search for “one more.”

If the scientist was right, at best I had five years to keep any human reason.

But that would take extraordinary luck, and the longer it went, the more I’d suffer like he had.

Realistically, the minimum was two years—so I should treat three years as my time limit.

“Damn… I should’ve eaten Spider-man when I had the… chance?”

Then I remembered.

Where had I made my base?

“Do Spider-man’s failed prototypes count?”

They should still be there.

I stood, went to the hidden backpack, and recovered my gear.

For now, I doused myself with water from the tank and wiped the blood off with a towel.

I poured water over my head again, regained my calm, and let out a long breath.

“Alright. First objective: return to base and eat one of Spider-man’s failed specimens. If that lets me see the third dream, good. If not…”

No—save that for if it happened.

I shouldered my pack, left the stadium, and ran for my base.

I considered saying at least a word to that woman from the Council, but assuming the worst, getting too involved wouldn’t end well.

I’d grown a bit too close to the elves, but with them, maybe—just maybe—I could still hope.

I ran.

Because there was still hope left—

For the record, my partner was still out and about.

I wanted that more than my voice.

+++

## 90 - A Certain Scientist’s Recording Log

Ah, ah—… mic test, mic test.

Yeah, it’s fine… it’s fine, right?

Budget constraints mean the gear’s a bit old… but spend too much and I can’t afford to live.

Honestly, war is one thing, but can’t someone do something about certain prices skyrocketing?

Even this room is… no, no—getting old makes me gripe too much.

Ah, the manual, the manual—wait, this is the wrong one.

What now…? Oh, but it’s the same series, so maybe it’ll work?

Yeah, yeah, this will do.

Hah… so that’s how the newer models are.

Nice that the controls are easier… oh, that feature isn’t on mine.

Maybe I should’ve forced it and brought the new rig… the picture’s supposed to be prettier, too.

Ah, if only I could’ve used the same studio as last time… but things never go how you want.

Okay, the script, the script… better check it just in case.

Right, all good.

Prep complete—then let’s start filming… hmm, after a drink of water.

“Now then—if you’re watching this, it means you’ve successfully acquired cells from the second subject. First, my compliments—congratulations. You likely remember the first recording already, so let me speak about my objective. It’s a very simple motive. Why did I do this? In a word: ‘revenge.’ The gene-modification technology was originally developed and tested by me. But someone made off with it. The result was military application, and thus monsters like you were born… Naturally, there were many failures and casualties. I wouldn’t have made such mistakes, but the youngster who stole my work found it a bit too heavy a load. To hide those failures, they spawned the so-called ‘Ultimate Lifeform Project’ from the altered tech. A laughable plan chasing a ‘strongest creature,’ and I intended to make it collapse. So I made one tweak to you lot. Simply put: those produced by the Genetically Enhanced Soldier Project can retain human reason for at most five years. That’s a projection—individual variance exists, but I’ll assert it won’t be exceeded by much. Environment and behavior also affect it, so assume a minimum of about one year. Since you’re being thrown into war, I expect you to lose reason and rampage around a year after deployment. When that happens, who’ll be sent against you? Fellow subjects, of course. This message is for the ones who kept devouring such berserk subjects. If you’ve seen hell, you should act just as I envisioned. One way or another, the thief will be punished. Ideally, if someone among you delivers the coup de grâce, it will prove the original technology is superior… but I’m not counting on it. It was my technology to begin with—then over-specced. I also gave you a perk: the more fellow subjects you eat, the more abilities you’ll awaken. So in terms of capability, it isn’t impossible… but, well, it’ll be hard. That about covers it—ah, one more thing… Even if you’ve shed your human form, if you wish to live on without losing a human heart, watch the third recording. I truly hope the day comes when we meet again.”

Phew, done and done.

I’ll have to check later to make sure it actually recorded.

Ah—this water hits the spot.

Come to think of it, it’s been a while since I talked this much.

What was that promise about “paying enough for you to live easy for life,” huh?

They cut me off after an advance that wouldn’t last ten years!

Thanks to that, even getting back into research work is hard!

And what’s this “we can take you for odd jobs” nonsense!

I had no money, so of course I had to nod along!

Damn it, if I was going to blow the whistle, I should’ve planned it better… botched that one.

Well, if I pull this off, that punk will at best be disgraced.

I should get a request to return to my post, too.

The problem is when they’ll deploy those assets… too soon and I can’t set the stage; too late and I run out of money.

If the Western Front’s going poorly, you’d expect more rumors to leak… Maybe they’ve got some other secret weapon?

Ugh, what to do about the third recording… I don’t exactly have spare cash… but if I don’t make it, it’ll come back to bite me… Eh? Time already?

Ah, I’m starting cleanup now—yes, yes, that’s right.

Right, no extension, please.

What to do about the third one… If I’m doing it, I need to do it soon… Bah, it’s all that punk’s fault to begin with!

It’s all because he didn’t pay what he promised!

Thanks to him I’ve ended up doing this!

And what is with this device, anyway!?

The imprinting window is way too short!

Is it a storage-capacity problem?

Or just underpowered hardware?

No, what’s the principle here in the first place?

Conditional memory recall is fine, but who knows how long it would take to reproduce with science.

I don’t understand it at all!

This is why I hate anything to do with magic!

Ah—sorry, I’ll be out right away.

Sigh… what am I going to do about that third recording…

+++

## 91

East of the elven domain—back in the underground facility of the former Fullretos Empire where Genetically Enhanced Soldiers were once experimented on, and which now served as my base.

There was much to think about on the road, but everything depended on the trial I was about to undertake.

First, however, came an obstacle: with this massive body of mine, I couldn’t move freely inside the facility. I had to destroy parts of the outer walls to make a path wide enough to pass.

And this was an underground facility—one wrong move could cause a collapse. I had to work carefully, and for that I needed internal information first.

I searched for a signboard to guide me, but before that, something caught my eye.

A box I didn’t recognize, with a letter stuck on top.

If anyone came here, it could only have been the elves.

As I suspected, it seemed Dame Oppai had revealed this location.

The trap notice was gone, so she herself may have visited too.

“Let’s deal with this first,” I muttered, stepping closer and taking the letter.

It said, in short: “I came to see you, but you weren’t home, so I left this note. The contents of the box are a gift.”

Opening it, I found it filled with fruit of every kind.

“Much appreciated,” I said, immediately grabbing one to bite into.

Its cool freshness was surely thanks to magic.

In times like this, I couldn’t help but admire: “Magic really is convenient.”

I finished the first apple and reached for a second, checking if anything else was inside—nothing.

It was only natural they had looked around here—but had they found my hidden treasures? I went to check.

The result… safe.

It seemed they refrained from the rude act of rummaging through someone else’s home.

I thought they might’ve taken things back, but aside from a single missing porno magazine on the bookshelf, nothing else had changed.

(Wait… who took that?)

I’d expected Number Six to visit—but no, clearly one of her escorts, a man, had secretly taken it.

“Well, in that case, so be it,” I decided. But it was possible Dame Oppai herself was the culprit.

Imagining how that might end brought an evil grin to my face, but I wasn’t in the mood to enjoy it.

Shifting my gaze deeper into the facility, I considered how to expand the narrow passageways.

(Spider-man was quite large himself… with legs spread, he’d be bigger than me. Wide corridors must have been his paths.)

What I needed was the facility’s signboard.

If I knew the destination and the passages, I could then widen them.

I soon found what I was looking for and began memorizing the facility’s layout.

But the chambers where test subjects likely slept were deep inside.

This would take time. Pulling down ceiling panels, I set to gouging the walls with brute strength.

Following the map, I avoided risky sections while expanding the halls.

As the passage widened steadily, my mind drifted into unwanted thoughts.

For instance, about that scientist—in the dream, he had said his motive was “revenge,” and stressed “casualties had occurred.”

(So, someone dear to him must have died, and he sought vengeance…?)

But the Empire itself was gone, let alone his enemy.

Everything ended in futility; the hatred remained two centuries, only to bring despair to the subjects.

The system, which made subjects fight each other and punish the thief of his technology, assumed they would face one another after losing reason.

But now that premise had crumbled, it no longer worked.

If I’d learned this within three months of awakening, like I had, there might still be time.

But what of him—the one who looked like an orc?

Already partly bereft of reason when I met him, when had he learned this truth?

Depending on the answer, it might be unbearably cruel.

(Could it be… that ruined city I saw…?)

If so, he had searched in the wrong place.

Worse, he had rallied forces and waged war against the Furon Council, showing his mind was far from sound.

Perhaps someone inherited the knowledge, but that was too risky a gamble.

Far better to scour the forest with hordes of orcs.

(In other words, he had been too far gone to even realize that… If he had been active for years, then losing all reason took time. A gradual erasure of self as a human. A cruel fate indeed. Was his hatred that deep?)

Realizing that he endured the terror of slowly losing himself, I resolved to avoid that fate at any cost.

My hands paused as I imagined the suffering of the one called Emperor.

Thinking of it striking me one day made my hands tremble—but my emotional control function suppressed it, letting me think clearly.

(Do everything possible. If it fails…)

Before the worst came, I would end it myself—or ask someone else.

That would surely mean relying on the elves.

I chuckled bitterly at how troublesome this overly durable body was, and returned to work.

Slow work, since I couldn’t do it sloppily.

Bit by bit, rubble piled up as I carved through the walls.

Carrying it back, I wished for proper tools.

And each time my eyes caught the box, I ended up grabbing more fruit.

Perhaps leaving it in plain sight wasn’t wise.

By the way, the note revealed Number Six’s real name.

She was “Rusheru Forshna.”

It gave me the same thrill as learning a girl’s email address in school.

My hand froze over the fourth fruit.

Was this her way of saying, “Come see me”?

Or perhaps it was an opening to ask the elves for help.

After all, I was the one who slew their greatest nemesis, the “Nightmare.” Surely they wouldn’t refuse me.

(Time-wise, I still have some leeway. Efficiency first, but consider contingencies. Contact them… No, not yet. Revealing myself now would be inconvenient.)

If I needed a pretext, the letter was enough.

And the woman from the Natural Harmony Committee would surely accept my request.

But if I asked a favor, I’d need a gift.

Though I hadn’t slain the Nightmare for them, knowing the truth left me feeling guilty, not proud.

I had no intention of revealing it, but I would make use of the fact.

“Best if it’s something material,” I murmured, stroking my chin, but as a man with a gray youth, nothing came to mind.

“Let’s at least search,” I thought, heading to the storeroom for something a woman might like. But of course, nothing of the sort.

Instead, I noticed the sugar I’d transferred to a container and never touched since.

Opening the lid, I saw movement writhing in the colored sugar. Silently, I closed it and pretended I hadn’t.

(Of course… it came from an undeveloped refinery. Leave it too long and this happens.)

I had checked it when I last left base for long, but… disappointing.

I picked up another item but again judged it unsuitable.

What would elves want, anyway?

As I rummaged, an idea struck.

“Yes. Books.”

All my books were Imperial-made—written in Furon language.

But that was perfect.

I knew someone fluent in Furon.

Likely the woman under Number Six—probably a headache for her.

Then I’d give her work.

Number Six would assign chores to an idler while gaining Imperial knowledge.

I’d be thanked, and my favor repaid—two birds with one stone.

And Dame Oppai would weep tears of joy at the blessing of labor.

“Brilliant,” I congratulated myself, browsing the shelf—only to find nothing suitable for women, or for elves in general.

If I gave them my collection, they’d recoil for sure.

Maybe I’d need to visit a town.

(No—there was somewhere else first.)

The Kingdom of Canaan—the place I killed Spider-man.

I had little hope, but I needed to see his corpse for myself.

+++

## 92

After one last check of my backpack to make sure I’d forgotten nothing, I slung it on and climbed up the elevator shaft to the surface.

Since I’d packed some fruit for snacks, the box was already less than half full.

So I transferred the rest into the cooler I kept at the base.

It was sweeter than expected and clearly high-quality—something premium—so I decided to eat it sparingly.

I locked up properly and set out—aiming northeast to enter the Kingdom of Canaan for now.

I roughly remembered the cave’s location.

But I didn’t know the exact spot where I’d killed Spider-man.

I only recalled a general direction and a vague distance; it had been the middle of the night, moon out or not.

I doubted I’d find any familiar landmarks, so I ran through the forest, ready for a difficult search.

Maybe because it had been a while since I’d headed north, my sense for the spacing of the trees lagged a bit.

After eating the second one—the Emperor—my abilities had improved across the board, and I needed to get used to that, too.

“Better slow down for a while,” I decided, running while keeping careful watch on my surroundings.

I might find an undiscovered lab, so scouting the forest mattered.

Crushing any goblins I spotted underfoot, I moved northeast—and came across a monster I’d never seen before.

“Oh… a pack of Forest Wolves. First time seeing them.”

In Imperial days they were rare, but seeing them in a pack took the charm away.

I remembered their faint green-tinged gray pelts being extremely valuable, and that overhunting had supposedly wiped them out within Imperial lands.

They were 1.5–2.0 meters long—bigger than normal wolves—recorded as highly aggressive toward other species, treating anything not their own as prey. I recalled hearing on TV that because they were often culled for that reason, the Empire had eradicated them.

In short—yeah, the wolves attacked.

Counting about seventeen in all, I figured “drop half and they’ll flee.” I snapped the neck of the first Forest Wolf that leapt with a speed-first short jab, then caught the one biting my ankle with my tail and smashed it into a tree.

Its fangs, buried in my leg, snapped with an audible crack as it went.

Naturally I was unhurt—or so I thought, until I later noticed a tiny mark left behind.

I grabbed one of the wolves that kept attacking and swung it like a weapon, knocking out about three. Deciding they couldn’t win, the rest began to fall back.

They withdrew once it was clear I wouldn’t pursue, and with only four dead—clean timing on their retreat.

Well-led packs were considered dangerous even in the Empire, so they’d probably serve as a good check on Canaan’s push south.

With that in mind, perhaps I shouldn’t cull too many monsters up north.

Except goblins. You’re a no.

Humanoid monsters sometimes had just enough brains to use Imperial relics.

And that’s not all.

Some of that trash destroyed things for fun without understanding their value—never mind not knowing how to use them.

So they couldn’t be left alive.

Exterminate on sight.

So I ran while doing pest control and watching the forest, and around sundown my senses finally started to settle in.

The problem was that night was coming, so I needed to slow down a bit.

Even with night vision, until I was fully adjusted, there was no guarantee I wouldn’t crash into something.

(Come to think of it, I always seem to pass through here at night.)

It might be good to scout this whole area once in daylight.

With those thoughts, I kept moving until sunrise.

Which brought me, after a long while, back to the Kingdom of Canaan.

Spotting a familiar highway, I first searched for the cave where I’d run into Spider-man.

At one point I wandered, wondering, “Did I go the wrong way?” but I eventually found the cave from back then—only to see people there.

And not thieves or the like—properly equipped, with a makeshift base of tents and the like set up around them.

(Don’t know the situation. Did something happen tied to that cave, and the kingdom’s doing something about it?)

It probably wasn’t about Spider-man, but it might complicate things a bit.

That said, I didn’t have business with the cave itself.

If they were operating around it, we might run into each other, but it shouldn’t be a problem.

“Right, a lot of people were abducted—they could be investigating that.”

I murmured softly and observed the makeshift base through gaps in the trees.

There was some distance, but I used my mimicry ability just in case, blended into the scenery as I closed in, and tried to pick up conversation… but they spoke less than expected.

Which suggested they weren’t a ragtag bunch—they looked like regular soldiers or something close.

I doubted they’d detect me, but with numbers you never knew what could happen.

I counted seven in visible range—so with people in the cave and tents, I assumed over twenty.

With that many, there might even be a mage.

I still wasn’t used to magic, so caution couldn’t hurt.

Inching closer and listening, all I heard was insects and leaves rubbing in the wind.

Even so, I lay in wait for any scrap of information—until three men emerged from the cave.

They stretched big and took deep breaths, like they were finally getting fresh air.

They seemed to be talking—but there I made a fatal mistake.

“—. ——, ——”

I couldn’t understand a word.

It was definitely the Canaan Kingdom language, but with the accent—and the speed—I couldn’t make it out at all.

(Crap. I caught a few words, but as sentences, none of it made sense.)

I could follow a bit of standard Canaanese, but even accounting for how fast they talked, this was a no-go.

I hadn’t planned for this.

“Looks like I should give up on intel-gathering here,” I decided, and started to slip away.

But then I heard the word “Argos” behind me, and the situation changed.

(No way… they don’t think I did the cave massacre, do they?)

Or maybe they intended to say as much.

Either way, things were drifting into an unfavorable state.

Granted, I was practically an enemy of Canaan to begin with. Still, false information spreading was unwelcome.

(And what can I do now…? Hand over the real culprit—rejected. I’m moving with that as my goal. Turning it over isn’t an option.)

So what then?

I kept my back to them and thought for a moment.

Conclusion—ignore it.

Thinking it through, I didn’t have the leeway to bother with Canaan.

For one, I’d killed that thing by tearing apart its human half and spider half.

Meaning it likely wouldn’t be recognized as the same individual, and nothing I did would clear suspicion off me anyway.

So I abandoned the idea of interfering and focused on my objective.

“Wasted enough time,” I muttered, widened the distance without being seen, dropped my mimicry, and started searching for the site where I killed Spider-man, relying on memory.

Around when the sun reached its zenith, I finally found the place where I’d finished things with him.

There, the word I’d left—“Die”—was still carved, proving this was the spot from that time.

But the key thing—the corpse of Spider-man—was nowhere to be found.

In fact, there wasn’t even a trace that a body had ever been here.

## 93

There were no traces, which in itself was strange.

The location was not mistaken.

Then why was the corpse gone?

Even if it had disappeared, there should at least have been some sign that a body had been there.

On this strange phenomenon, I could reach only two conclusions.

The first: genetically enhanced soldiers vanish completely after death—measures taken, perhaps, to prevent the leakage of technology.

The second: outside interference.

In other words, someone removed the corpse and concealed the fact that anything had ever been here.

It had to be one of the two, but there was no way the body could naturally disintegrate without a trace.

If the first were true, it would simply mean I had wasted my time.

But if the second were the case, I couldn’t predict what consequences might follow.

That said, would anyone who saw a human upper body and a headless giant spider truly conclude that they had once been one creature?

Unless they had seen it before with their own eyes, they would at most connect it as “a devoured human and a dead spider.”

The truth would never come close, but this raised another point of suspicion.

The soldiers at the cave.

Why was such a disciplined and highly motivated unit being deployed to such a remote location?

What if they had secured the body and erased all signs of it?

The more I thought about it, the more unsettling it became.

Technical leaks should have been impossible.

But I could not allow Spider-Man’s corpse to be exploited.

After all, I had nearly been killed once by his poison.

His acid and webs, too, had been effective against me—if those were replicated, or something similar created, the consequences would be dangerous.

(Should I lament how troublesome this is… or be glad I learned of it beforehand?)

The best case would be if the Empire’s splendid technology had already disposed of everything.

But “betray expectations at the crucial moment” was practically a national specialty of my homeland.

Having reached the conclusion that “I need to confirm this,” I let out a long sigh.

Suspicion pointed above all to the cave.

Its security could not be called airtight, but for such a remote place it was absurdly over-guarded, practically screaming “please doubt us.”

The question now was how to deal with them.

I was already an enemy of the Kingdom of Canaan, so that much was no longer the issue.

But if I raised my threat level too far and forced them to form alliances with other nations, that would be troublesome.

As it stood, this should not be a problem—but what if I killed a noble or an important figure among them?

If the elves were prompted to move in response, that would be disastrous.

Unlikely, yes—but not impossible.

More than anything, if the elves who had carried back reports about Spider-Man learned of this, what would happen?

“I can’t move too loudly…”

In a single phrase, this situation was “a real pain.”

If I left it alone, the risk was great.

If I interfered, I risked suspicion of being connected to Spider-Man.

And though I did have camouflage abilities, in a cave’s confined space my bulk made hiding almost impossible.

Resolving this peacefully without being discovered was a hopeless prospect.

So instead, I would resort to violent resolution without being discovered.

In short: I was ready to risk exposing my trump card, the “camouflage ability,” for this stealth mission.

It was a bitter choice, but when weighing best and worst outcomes, it was not a bad gamble.

As long as I avoided the worst, the balance would lean positive.

The best case: Spider-Man’s corpse was preserved, and I succeeded in stealing it without being discovered.

Then I could devour it and experience the third dream.

The worst case: the corpse wasn’t there, my camouflage was exposed, survivors carried the report back, and suspicions about me spread.

If elves added their testimony, my ties to the Empire would be exposed.

Then I would surely become the enemy of the entire world, and even my survival would be in doubt.

But in truth, if I did nothing, the time limit would come and the same thing would happen anyway—only sooner or later.

So I set my resolve.

I returned to the cave, activated my camouflage, and used tents for cover as I closed in, mapping out every soldier’s position and distance.

(Thirteen soldiers outside in total… fewer than expected, but knocking them out or killing them without anyone inside the cave noticing—that’s no easy feat, is it?)

Their positioning was particularly bad.

Not everyone was scattered: three were resting inside tents, three tents in all.

Two stood at the cave entrance.

The last two patrolled separately around the cave.

So I decided to start by silently taking down the patrols.

I crept up, struck their necks with hand chops to feign knocking them out, but actually killed them.

Then I approached the two at the entrance, threw a stone to distract them, and when they turned, punched one in the gut.

He flew backward, crashing into the other, and by bad luck struck his head on a sharp rock, dying instantly.

The other’s abdomen was wrecked—he died as well.

Before any alarm could spread, I rushed to the tents.

But they had heard the sound—“What’s going on!?” one shouted, stepping out.

I forced him back inside with brute strength, and when he fell, he struck his head hard and was knocked out.

I couldn’t let them see me, so I struck from behind the tent cloth, killing the remaining two.

One died of blunt trauma, another from a caved-in skull.

That cleared one tent.

I moved to the next.

They had been asleep, so they came out slowly, and I cut them down before they could even react.

Blood spread beneath the collapsed tent, but still no one emerged from the final one.

So I crushed it outright, with the same results.

For some reason, none of the corpses wore pants.

In the end, one survivor remained.

To remove any risk, I snapped his neck and posed him in a “combat death” position to cover my tracks.

I tried the same with the other corpses—placing weapons in their hands—but it was hardly convincing.

(Well, at least I’ve learned that with my increased strength, holding back has become even harder. That’s something.)

Consoling myself, I moved into the cave.

Inside, there were fewer people than expected.

I made it deep without much resistance—only three deaths inside the cave.

But even with camouflage, the torchlight made movement risky, since shadows gave me away.

So my options were only striking from behind, or ambush.

Still, I met little resistance and pressed on, until I saw the place where the back door had been.

The rubble had been cleared, but the massive hole I had made remained.

Inside the room, three men stood.

And on the table before them lay the preserved human half of Spider-Man.

# Striving For The Luxury Liner – Vol 23 - **20 – Blind Spot**

The mermaid royalty talks directly to the Sea God-sama, and the dark elves have seen letters from the Goddess of the Forest-sama.

But Alessia-san and the others were so cute during those five days!

Camille-san said confidently.

While we were busy camping and cooking meals in the Southern City, Camille-san made various arrangements. The two ferries I own are now fully loaded, including the parking spaces.

She is planning to gather more supplies in this country and fill the unused spaces on the ferries and luxury liners to transport them; even the empty spaces in the rooms are packed tightly.

If the ship wasn’t designed to stay afloat, it would probably sink under the weight of all that cargo. Buying a transport ship would be even more impressive, but that would mean a huge increase in work, so I’m keeping that idea to myself.

I told her it wasn’t necessary to go that far. I got the impression that the God of Commerce-sama would be satisfied with a certain amount of supplies.

The role is to provide the water to fill the barrels, so I think transporting enough supplies to prevent the trade network in the southern continent from collapsing would be sufficient.

I explained this to her, but she said, “It’s not that we can’t make a profit, right? I don’t intend to make excessive profits, but I don’t see any reason to give up profits when there’s such an obvious business opportunity in the form of a supply shortage.”

Well, that’s true.

In this case, it’s not a matter of charity or rescue; it’s strictly a business matter.

I feel sorry for the people caught up in the war. However, I think God intends for the people living in that country or on that continent to solve the disasters that occur there. Therefore, it’s not our place to step in and help.

Alessia-san and the others would help people in need if they encountered them, but that’s within their personal discretion.

I can’t do it. I might want to help if I saw people in need, but seeing a place ravaged by war is scary enough that I’d definitely hide on the ship. Wait, I remember we needed an interpreter on the other continent. Ines and Felicia learned basic greetings and everyday conversation, but not enough to do business.

Am I going to be dragged into this?

No, no, no—I don’t want that! I’m just an ordinary, cowardly person. I can handle normal business negotiations, but doing business in a war-torn country is too scary for me.

I’m an interpreter, but I want to choose the time and place. When it comes to business, I’m in charge, and I take pride in my work, so I’ll do my best. I’ll try my best, even if I’m scared. However, I couldn’t handle rescuing people who’ve been victimized.

Especially if I see children suffering. Yes, I would definitely mess up.

I messed up with the children at the orphanage, and I ended up making it like a fortress. If I were to witness a more tragic scene, I couldn’t imagine what I’d do.

I’m faced with an unexpected dilemma. As I think about it, it seems that Camille-san and Alessia-san are discussing something.

We have to take action now. It’s best to nip the danger in the bud.

“Excuse me, how many people here understand the language of the southern continent?”

Silence falls over the room.

Camille-san mutters, her expression serious. She had been assigned to stay here for the king’s reception, and Donatella-san had been chosen to accompany us.

We planned to meet her when we received the supplies in Cagliari.

Even among Girasole, only Ilma-san had a slight understanding of it. Had I not noticed, it would have caused serious issues with my work.

Learning the language was difficult in the Western continent, but doing so in a disaster area would be a whole other level of difficulty.

That’s good. Unlike the western continent, which was completely cut off from the rest of the world and unknown, the southern continent is connected to us through the pepper trade, so it seems we can obtain materials there.

Then we’ll be fine.

We’re not leaving right away, and the voyage will take some time.

It’s not enough time to learn a language, of course, but we have Ilma-san.

Just as she did with the Western Continent, she will be able to master the language quickly.

If the other members focus on specific fields, they won’t need much interpretation from me.

It shouldn’t take long to get to Cagliari on the Galette. We need to get her to focus on her studies as soon as possible, or I’ll have a lot more work to do when I arrive.

That means more work for Mauro-san.

I don’t want to push an elderly person too hard, but he’s energetic and in good shape, so I’m sure he’ll be fine.

Ines and the other boat-driving enthusiasts look eager to go, too.

That was all I needed to say; they started deciding the order right away. They’re definitely planning to race.

They’ll probably try a time trial between Lucca and Cagliari.

While I was thinking about how busy I was going to be, Ilma-san approached me with a seductive smile.

I was supposed to focus on reviewing the documents from Saporabi and the Western Continent, but Ilma-san is so curious that she’s borderline crazy. She couldn’t possibly be uninterested in the southern continent, even if the connection is tenuous.

She already seems to have learned the language to some extent on her own, so she’ll probably master it faster than she did the language of the western continent.

Studying with Ilma-san, whose curiosity is so evident, will be difficult, but…

It’s hard to resist lust. I understand it’s hard, but when I’m alone with her in a closed room and she shows her innocent side and unguarded behavior, which she doesn’t usually display, I can’t resist.

I’ll probably regret it sometimes.

For now, though, I’ll do my best to prepare so that I can seclude myself when we go to the southern continent.

Donatella’s POV:

He possesses unparalleled power, immense wealth, and a formidable military force, yet he remains humble and kind to his subordinates. **22 – Saporabi Conference**

I feel a little pressure being their teacher, but having three beautiful women as students makes it worthwhile, so I don’t think it’s so bad.

A heated discussion is taking place right in front of me.

The participants are me, Alessia-san, Camille-san, Donatella-san, Mauro-san, and the three Saporabis.

Alessia-san and I are keeping a low profile, while Mauro-san is trying to calm the discussion and act as a moderator. Camille-san, Donatella-san, and the three Saporabis are engaged in a fierce debate.

They are discussing the timing of Saporabi’s consciousness liberation on the Castle.

Camille-san emphasizes the importance of making an impact and wants to do it on the first regular voyage. She wants the consciousness liberation to be an event held after the ceremony, once the current Saporabis have gotten used to their new lives. Donatella-san emphasizes the importance of repeating the pre-introduction to improve completion levels.

The discussion cannot reach a conclusion because the three Saporabis are divided in their opinions.

One supports Camille-san, one supports Donatella-san, and one is neutral.

You might wonder why they are divided even though they are the same Saporabi. However, even though they are the same Saporabi, they have different souls. Liberating their consciousness means liberating their individuality, so it is natural that they have different opinions.

If their opinions were more evenly split, it would be easier to reach a conclusion. However, the neutral Saporabi complicates matters. Mauro-san is in the same position, but he is also the moderator and must remain neutral.

Incidentally, Alessia-san and I initially expressed our opinions to a certain extent and then went with the flow.

Once opinions began to diverge, attempts were made to persuade us to join their side. Halfway through, the persuasion turned into brainwashing, so we quickly gave up and became observers.

That’s why Alessia-san and I are just observers now.

I still didn’t expect the discussion about Saporabi to heat up at this point, though.

The rebellion had little impact here, allowing us to gather supplies smoothly. All that was left was to depart for the southern continent.

Assuming I can’t come up with a solution on the spot, the important thing is that I don’t get involved in the meeting. In other words, if the meeting is postponed and a vague conclusion is reached, that’s fine.

After all, I’m not interested in profit.

I’m good at self-preservation. I can shift the focus of the problem. Camille-san and Donatella-san are talking about Saporabi. Okay, I’ve decided.

“We don’t yet have a complete understanding of Saporabi’s movements now that their consciousness has been freed. Shouldn’t we focus on that first?”

Everyone turned to look at me in surprise at my sudden outburst. I had been keeping a low profile, but they didn’t have to be so shocked. Especially you, Saporabi. I’m your employer—or rather, your summoner. I did kill you all, but…

It’s confusing how life works, isn’t it? I slaughtered you because you were profitable and delicious, and now you’re my subordinates. It’s all the Creator God-sama’s fault.

“Wataru-san, do you have any ideas?”

As I was thinking about it, Camille-san asked me a question. I wish she wouldn’t look at me with such an expectant expression.

“Well, it’s not a groundbreaking idea, but as a trial, I thought we could free the consciousness of the Saporabi assigned exclusively to Camille-san and the others. While you’re at it, give them names to deepen your relationship. While we’re at it, how about making those three Saporabi your subordinates?”

The three Saporabi are business-minded and talented, but honestly, dealing with them and their ideas is a hassle. I want to stay on this luxury liner and live an elegant life, even if it means I’m not productive.

“What? Is that okay?”

“That’s a wonderful suggestion.”

“Yes, I’d be happy if those children were conscious of their roles. Those three Saporabi are excellent and will definitely be a big help.”

The response was more favorable than I had expected. I had assigned them exclusively to support Camille-san and the others in their commercial activities. However, it seems that they were treated not only as subordinates but also were loved more than that.

After all, they are big stuffed animals that can move and help out. If I didn’t know the circumstances, I could understand why they would be so fond of them.

If we can set sail while they’re distracted by the Saporabi, Camille-san and Mauro-san can take care of things while we’re away.

“It’s decided. Wataru-san, please keep it like this.”

“I’ll take this one.”

“I’ll go with this one.”

The comment was intended to derail the meeting. As expected, the meeting ended inconclusively. However, Camille-san and the others started thinking about settings for their Saporabi, which caused the meeting to drag on even longer.

I suggested they think about it individually and reconvene the next day, but they kept putting it off, saying, “Just a little longer” and “Just a little more.” We ended up wasting over an hour.

Camille-san and the others were concerned about compatibility with the new Saporabi. Based on their interactions with the existing Saporabi, they had various thoughts and were particular about the settings.

I regret not continuing the meeting as usual.

“I understand your requests. However, I can’t guarantee that everything will be exactly as you want it to be, except for the names. Their personalities and preferences are entirely up to Saporabi.”

After all, it’s a liberation of consciousness. I don’t decide their will or personalities. As for their costumes, I can get a general idea when I summon them, but I won’t know the details.

Actually, the liberated Saporabi change their costumes to whatever they like. I don’t mind, as long as they don’t wear costumes that ruin the atmosphere of the ship. But I’m secretly curious about how they change their costumes.

“I understand. Please do your best, Wataru-san.”

Since you asked me to do my best, please don’t put too much pressure on me with your intense gaze.

“…I’ll give it a try.”

I read the specifications—or rather, the character sheets—submitted by Camille-san and the others. I recalled the Saporabis assigned exclusively to them and summoned them again.

While I’m at it, I’ll re-summon one of the three Saporabis and send the other two back. These three will be assigned to support Camille-san and the others.

“Kyaa! How cute, Josephine! Oh, Alan is cool—or rather, serious. But it suits him.”

Camille-san hugged them before I could check their personalities, but it seems that I was able to summon them as requested.

Her name is Josephine. She’s a female Saporabi wearing a white sailor uniform. One of them is named Alan. He wears black-rimmed glasses and old-fashioned bank clerk clothes. He looks serious but seems good at negotiation.

It would be scary to be forced into negotiations while dressed like that. It would feel like being scolded by an accountant. Well, I’ve never had to settle expenses before, though.

I wanted to check for problems, but that seems impossible right now, so I’ll continue summoning Donatella-san and Mauro-san’s Saporabi.

“Fufu, Sophia and Lorenzo, you look great together. I look forward to working with you in the future.”

Donatella-san greets them calmly, but her hands are shaking so much that it ruins the moment. If no one were watching, I would probably hug her and stroke her hair like I did with Camille-san.

Actually, I couldn’t resist and started stroking her hair.

Sophia and Lorenzo are wearing ribbed clothing with gold decorations that seem a bit flashy. I thought ribbed clothing looked better on slim people, but it suits the stocky bodies of the Saporabi somehow. It’s not bad, but it looks a little stiff.

Next, I summoned Mauro-san’s Saporabi.

“Ugh, why!”

Mauro-san cried out in surprise when he saw the Saporabi that appeared.

It was probably because the Saporabi that appeared was wearing the default sailor outfit. I had a feeling that would happen. It seems that the Saporabi rejected Mauro-san’s request after all.

I thought it might end up like this, if only a little.

It would be a relief if Mauro-san just gave up and accepted it, but it doesn’t seem likely. I still can’t go back to my room. I want Rimu and Pent to heal me.

+++

**23 – Selfishness in Full Swing**

After finishing our business in Lucca, we moved on to Cagliari, where our work was going smoothly and we had some breathing room. However, we were divided on the issue of Saporabi consciousness liberation, so we decided to put it off for the time being. I suggested that Camille-san and the others customize their assigned Saporabi, but that would take more time. There were also some issues with Mauro-san’s Saporabi.

The Saporabi’s costume change failed, and, watching Mauro-san in shock, I secretly agreed with the outcome.

From the moment the character designs were submitted, I had a feeling this would happen. In fact, I would have been more surprised if things had gone smoothly.

The designs were so extreme that they went beyond gothic lolita and into sweet lolita, with an extra dose of sweetness added for good measure.

I couldn’t imagine a man of Mauro-san’s age liking such designs.

Well, to each their own, so maybe an old man with grandchildren could like sweet lolita fashion after all.

Hmm, I don’t know.

I didn’t know anything about Mauro-san’s personal Saporabi, but I expected the Saporabi with a businessperson’s personality to reject that costume. I can’t imagine a profit-oriented businessman and sweet Lolita fashion together.

“Mauro-san, why did you choose this design in the first place? It doesn’t seem like the kind of thing you would like.”

I asked Mauro-san, who was in a state of shock.

“I thought my great-grandchild would like it.”

What?

When I ask him about it, he tells me that, while I wasn’t there, his grandchild brought his great-grandchild to visit the Castle.

The great-grandchild is still very young and was scared of Mauro-san for some reason. They wouldn’t get close to him at all.

Mauro-san was quietly shocked by this and remembered that his great-grandchild was interested in Saporabi. He thought that if he showed them the cutest Saporabi, they would be impressed and respect him.

That’s selfish.

As long as he does his job properly, I have no problem with it. However, Mauro-san, who created a design that goes beyond sweet and cute for his great-grandchild, is quietly amazing.

He was rejected by the Saporabi, but he may be praised as godlike in some circles.

Still, great-grandchildren… In this world, people tend to marry young, so it’s normal for someone like Mauro-san to have great-grandchildren.

It’s a harsh world, so it makes sense that people would be eager to have children.

However, adventurers tend to marry late.

Given the dangerous nature of their profession, you’d think they’d want to have children. But the risk of dying and leaving an orphan behind, or the difficulty of moving or going on expeditions, seems to contribute to the trend of adventurers marrying later.

Adventurers are a low-tier profession, so it’s probably unavoidable.

Adventurers who aren’t just doing it as a hobby can earn a lot of money if they make it to the top. However, when they’re starting out, they risk their lives for low pay in extremely harsh conditions.

It’s difficult to start a family on a low salary. Once they make it to the top, they level up and live longer. This makes them less likely to rush into marriage, allowing them to enjoy their time.

Practical adventurers seem to find safe ways to earn a living in the middle ranks and start families. However, the dilemma is that practical types don’t tend to become adventurers in the first place.

Adventurers who are driven or do it for fun have a tough job. I think Alessia-san and the others are doing it 70% for fun. They probably became adventurers because they longed for travel and freedom. With their beauty, they could have had many other options for earning a living besides becoming adventurers.

I am convinced that my Ines is a 100% hobbies-driven adventurer.

“Wataru? You understand how this poor old man feels, don’t you?”

Before I knew it, I had drifted from thinking about the marriage customs of this world to thinking about the joys and sorrows of adventurers. However, Mauro-san’s pity-inducing question brought me back to reality.

“Mauro-san, even if you call yourself a pitiful old man, you’re quite wealthy. You’ve leveled up so much that you’re just a little old. I can’t feel sorry for you.”

He may have had his struggles, but Mauro-san is an overwhelmingly successful man. Thanks to his level-ups, he’s so energetic that I wonder if he’s really an old man. Honestly, if it weren’t for my cheat ability, I’d be jealous.

He says he’s a pitiful old man, but he really just wants to use Saporabi because he wants to be loved by his great-grandchildren.

“You’re so cold. Can’t you do something about it?”

Mauro-san tries to act sulky, but he’s not cute at all. I don’t want to get involved with him.

“It seems that you can negotiate to some extent with the Saporabi who have had their consciousness liberated. So, it depends on how hard Mauro-san tries, doesn’t it?”

I don’t understand it very well yet, but it seems that they’re allowed a certain degree of freedom as long as they don’t deviate from their original duties.

However, the skills in this world are unfriendly. It would be nice if, like with the ship summoning staff, you could learn about a skill once you’ve acquired it. But there’s no such follow-up, so you have to learn by trial and error.

That’s why it’s common not to know how to utilize unique skills. There’s a huge difference between the good and bad ones.

Actually, I guess the correct answer is that you can’t judge whether a skill is good or bad because you can’t use it properly.

I’m curious about how the person from the Empire, who possesses the laser beam’s unique skill, learned to use it.

In this world, you wouldn’t understand what a laser is.

Huh? The merchant-like Saporabi heard me, took out a piece of paper, and started writing. Don’t ask me where he got the paper. It’s pointless. This ship delivers food and alcohol upon request, so it’s too late now.

Saporabi handed the paper to Mauro-san. Is it just my imagination, or does he look vaguely satisfied?

“What’s this? Compensation for work outside your main job? In other words, you’ll be paid to entertain my great-grandchildren? But isn’t entertaining the passengers who boarded the ship your main job?”

I don’t know what Saporabi’s original job was, but the merchant Saporabi will do whatever you ask in exchange for payment.

However, as Mauro-san said, helping customers is literally part of Saporabi’s job.

Saporabi tapped on the part that Mauro-san hadn’t read yet.

“Hmm, so basically, if I pay the fee written here, you’ll work to make my great-grandchild respect me? I see. In that case, it’s worth paying an extra fee. But isn’t the price a bit steep?”

Saporabi’s demand must have sparked Mauro-san’s merchant spirit because negotiations suddenly began.

“Let’s call this meeting adjourned and wrap things up…”

I said in a low voice, and I fled the room with Alessia-san. Camille-san and Donatella-san remained behind, admiring their respective Saporabi, while Mauro-san was engrossed in negotiations.

**＊＊＊**

Well, it’s about time to set sail for the southern continent.

We decided not to stop at Dark Elf Island this time, so we’re heading straight there.

I asked Felicia if we should stop at the island, since we had some time, but she said we should prioritize this job.

I suppose she thought she couldn’t prioritize personal matters because this job is related to a god. That’s typical of Felicia’s thoughtful nature.

Come to think of it, this will be my first long voyage with Donatella-san, won’t it?

The only other time I traveled by ship with her was when we were power leveling and moving to Cagliari. So, I think this will be our first pure voyage.

That is, if we can call this trip “pure.” I’ll plan events for us to enjoy on the ship. Swimsuit events are a must.

As for raising awareness of the Saporabi, it hasn’t been resolved, but it has been put on hold.

The Saporabi had their consciousness liberated more than I had imagined. They were capable, unique, and highly independent.

This inspired the entrepreneurial spirit of Camille-san, Donatella-san, and Mauro-san, who were in charge of verification. We were able to buy time until the preparations for departure to the southern continent were complete.

Verification will probably continue during the trip, and by the time we return, the plan will be perfected.

There are also things I’ve come to realize at this stage.

When talented people support each other, it’s like cheating.

Well, that was amazing.

Camille-san, Donatella-san, and Mauro-san are talented individuals who gave various instructions to the Saporabi for verification.

The Saporabi, unable to demonstrate their talents under me, responded perfectly to the instructions.

Then, Camille-san and the others gave more challenging instructions, and the Saporabi responded. This process repeated itself, and even from the sidelines, efficiency itself seemed to be embodied as preparations progressed.

Still, transporting cargo and dealing with issues outside the Castle were difficult, so it took time. Since the outside of the ship is outside the Saporabi’s support range, there was nothing that could be done about it.

Nevertheless, they made significant contributions by proposing transportation plans and devising solutions to problems.

They were supposed to be the souls of horned rabbits. Why are they so talented? I’ll ask the Creator God-sama when I get the chance.

It’s strange that the same horned rabbit that was ferocious and only wanted to attack me is now so competent.

Oh, everyone has come out onto the deck. Now is a good time to set sail.

“Everyone, we’re about to set sail. We’ll head out to the open sea on the Lutto, and then we’ll transfer to another ship to go straight to the southern continent. Does anyone have anything they’ve forgotten?”

Everyone responds that they’re ready. Let’s go!

“We’re setting sail!”

I wave goodbye to Camille-san and the others who came to see us off as the Lutto speeds away.

The southern continent… I hope we don’t get into any trouble.

++++
